Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Wife's Response: 'Abantu Nafsī' vs 'Akhtartu Nafsī' in Divorce Delegation
Ruling on alternative responses to divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife responds 'abantu nafsi' to 'talliqi nafsik', distinguish it from 'akhtartu nafsi', and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abantu nafsi triggers divorce; akhtartu nafsi does not—only words synonymous with talaq are effective in delegation.

When a husband delegates divorce to his wife by saying 'talliqi nafsik', her response determines whether a divorce occurs. The Hidayah presents a nuanced ruling: words like 'abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself) count as talaq, while 'akhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself) do not. However, Imam Abu Hanifa held a differing view on 'abantu nafsi', leading to an intra-madhhab dispute. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two possible responses
The husband uses tafweed (delegation): 'talliqi nafsik'. The wife may reply with 'abantu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi'. The legal effect of each is different.
2
State the ruling for 'abantu nafsi' according to the Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs. The extra attribute of ba'in (immediate severance) is ignored as superfluous.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State the ruling for 'akhtartu nafsi'
No divorce occurs because 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of talaq. It only causes divorce when it is a response to explicit takhyeer (choice-giving), which 'talliqi nafsik' is not.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's opposing view on 'abantu nafsi'
Abu Hanifa holds that 'abantu nafsi' does not result in divorce because 'ibanah' is distinct from talaq; the wife brought something other than what was delegated.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn: a revocable divorce occurs. The practical effect is that the wife is divorced during the iddah, and the husband may take her back within that period.
This ruling is consistent with the broader Hanafi treatment of ibanah as a talaq word.

تشریح مسئلہ اگر مرد نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا طلقی نفسک عورت نے جواب میں بسنت نفسی کہا تو اس صورت میں طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی ۔ اور اگر جواب میں اخترت نفسی کہا تو طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ ان دونوں کے درمیان وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ ابانت الفاظ طلاق میں سے ہے۔ چنانچہ شوہر نے اگر اپنی بیوی سے بنیت طلاق اب تک کہا یا عورت نے ابنت نفسی کہا۔ اور شوہر نے اجزت ذالک کہہ دیا تو عورت مطلقہ بائنہ ہو جائے گی ۔ پس اس صورت میں عوت کا کلام ابنت نفسی شوہر کے کلام طلقی نفسک کے اصل طلاق میں
Husband said 'talliqi nafsik', wife said 'abantu nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi' — from which word does divorce occur? And if he said to his wife 'talliqi nafsik' and she said 'abantu nafsi', she becomes divorced (taliq). And if she said 'I have chosen myself', she is not divorced, because 'ibanah' is from the words of talaq. Do you not see that if he said 'abantuki' intending talaq, or she said 'abantu nafsi' and the husband said 'I allow that', she becomes ba'in. So it corresponds to the delegation (tafweed) in essence, except that she added an attribute, namely 'ta'jil al-ibanah' (immediate separation). The extra attribute is void, and the original (talaq raj'i) is established, just as if she said 'tallaqtu nafsi talaqatan ba'inatan' — the expected ruling is that a revocable divorce occurs. In contrast to 'ikhtiyar', because it is not from the words of talaq. Do you not see that if he said to his wife 'akhtartuki' or 'ikhtari' intending talaq, it does not occur. And if she spontaneously said 'akhtartu nafsi' and the husband said 'I allow', nothing occurs, except that it is known as talaq by consensus (ijma') when it is in response to takhyeer. His saying 'talliqi nafsik' is not takhyeer, so it is void. And from Abu Hanifa it is narrated that nothing occurs by her saying 'abantu nafsi', because she brought something other than what was delegated, since 'ibanah' is different from talaq. (Urdu translation follows, then clarification:) Clarification: If the husband said to his wife 'talliqi nafsik' and she responded with 'abantu nafsi', a revocable divorce occurs. And if she responded with 'akhtartu nafsi', no divorce occurs. The difference is that 'ibanah' is among the words of talaq. ...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute within the Hidayah concerning the wife's response when delegated to divorce herself. The relied-upon position (of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that if she says 'abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself), a revocable divorce occurs because 'ibanah' is a synonym for talaq. Her additional description of 'ba'in' is considered superfluous and ignored, so the original revocable divorce stands. However, if she says 'akhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself), no divorce occurs because 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of talaq; it is only effective when it comes as a response to explicit takhyeer (giving a choice), which is not the case here. Abu Hanifa held a differing view: even 'abantu nafsi' does not result in divorce because it is different from talaq. The mufta bihi (operative) position is that of the Sahibayn, as it is more in line with general usage of divorce terminology.

Key Terms
ابنت (abantu): A verbal form meaning 'I have separated/made ba'in' — considered a word of talaq in the Hanafi school.
اخترت (akhtartu): 'I have chosen' — not a word of talaq unless in response to explicit takhyeer.
ابانت (ibanah): Making ba'in; a synonym for talaq, especially divorce that severs marital ties.
تفويض (tafweed): Delegation by the husband to the wife to divorce herself.
تخيير (takhyeer): Giving the wife a choice (e.g., 'choose yourself'), which if accepted leads to talaq by consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsik', and she responds 'akhtartu nafsi'. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the result?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) Three divorces.
C) No divorce.
D) One irrevocable divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a wife's response to divorce delegation must use a recognised word of talaq; 'abantu nafsi' is effective (revocable divorce), while 'akhtartu nafsi' is not.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Wife's Response 'Abantu Nafsi' vs 'Ikhtartu Nafsi' to Husband's 'Tilliqi Nafsaki'
Ruling on validity of divorce by wife's response with separation or choice words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings of 'Abantu nafsi' and 'Ikhtartu nafsi' in response to 'Tilliqi nafsaki', explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Words of separation like 'abanah' effect divorce when delegated; words of choice like 'ikhtiyar' do not, except in true takhyir.

When a husband delegates divorce by saying 'Tilliqi nafsaki', he gives his wife ownership of the divorce act. Her response determines whether a divorce occurs. This lesson examines the linguistic and legal distinctions between two possible responses, and the notable intra-Hanafi dispute on one of them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation
Recognize that 'Tilliqi nafsaki' is a form of tamlik (making the wife owner of the divorce), not takhyir (choice). The husband does not give her a choice between options; he commands her to divorce herself.
Tamlik is binding and confined to the session.
2
Analyse the wife's response 'Abantu nafsi'
The word 'abanah' (separation) is considered a word of divorce. Even though the wife adds a description (hastening), the description is disregarded and the original divorce is established. Thus, a revocable divorce occurs according to the Sahibayn.
The added description is 'تعجيل الابانة' (hastening the separation).
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'Abantu nafsi' does not effect divorce because 'ibanah' is not synonymous with divorce in his view. The wife brought something other than what was delegated.
Abu Hanifa's argument: 'الابانة تغاير الطلاق' (separation differs from divorce).
4
Apply the analysis to 'Ikhtartu nafsi'
The word 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is not a word of divorce. Even if the husband says 'Ikhtartuki' meaning divorce, it does not effect divorce. However, if the husband gives a true takhyir (e.g., 'Ikhtari' meaning 'choose between staying and divorce'), and she says 'Ikhtartu nafsi', divorce occurs by ijma. But 'Tilliqi nafsaki' is not takhyir.
The exception is based on the event of takhyir with the Companions.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: 'Abantu nafsi' results in a revocable divorce. Abu Hanifa's view is a weak narration.
The author of the Hidayah presents the Sahibayn's view as the operative one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان قال لها طلقی نفسك فقالت ابنت نفسى طلقت ولوقالت قد اخترت نفسى لم تطلق لان الابانة من الفاظ الطلاق الاترى انه لو قال ابنتک ینوی به الطلاق او قالت ابنت نفسى فقال الزوج قداجزت ذالك بانت فكانت موافقة للتفويض فى الاصل الا انها زادت فيه وصفا وهو تعجيل الابانة فيلغو الوصف الزائد وثبت الاصل كما اذاقالت طلقت نفسى تطليقة بائنة وينبغى ان يقع تطليقة رجعية بخلاف الاختيار لانه ليس من الفاظ الطلاق الأترى انه لو قال لامرأته اخترتک او اختارى ينوى الطلاق لم يقع ولوقالت ابتداء اخترت نفسى فقال الزوج اجزت لا يقع شيء الا انه عرف طلاقا بالاجماع اذا حصل جوابا للتخيير وقوله طلقی نفسك ليس بتخيير فيلغو وعن ابي حنيفة انه لا يقع شيء بقولها ابنت نفسى لانها اتت بغير ما فوض اليها اذالابانة تغاير الطلاق
And if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself,' and she said: 'I have separated myself,' then she is divorced. And if she said: 'I have chosen myself,' then she is not divorced, because al-ibanah (separation) is from the words of divorce. Do you not see that if he said: 'I have separated you' intending divorce, or she said: 'I have separated myself' and the husband said: 'I have permitted that,' she becomes separated (bainah). So it is in accordance with the delegation (tafwid) in the original, except that she added a description, which is hastening the separation. So the added description is disregarded, and the original is established, as if she said: 'I have divorced myself with one final divorce (taliqa bainah),' but it should be a revocable divorce (taliqa raj'iyyah). Contrary to ikhtiyar (choice), because it is not from the words of divorce. Do you not see that if he said to his wife: 'I have chosen you' or 'Choose,' intending divorce, it does not occur. And if she said initially: 'I have chosen myself' and the husband said: 'I have permitted,' nothing occurs. However, it is recognized as divorce by consensus (ijma) when it occurs in response to takhyir (giving choice), and his statement 'Divorce yourself' is not takhyir, so it is disregarded. And from Abu Hanifa: nothing occurs by her saying 'I have separated myself,' because she brought other than what was delegated to her, since al-ibanah differs from divorce.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a husband tells his wife 'Tilliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) and she responds with either 'Abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself) or 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself). The main ruling is that 'Abantu nafsi' results in a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), while 'Ikhtartu nafsi' does not result in divorce. The reasoning is that 'abanah' (separation) is among the words that can effect divorce, even when used metaphorically, as evidenced by the cases where a husband says 'Abantuk' intending divorce, or a wife says 'Abantu nafsi' and the husband approves—then separation occurs. In the original delegation (tafwid), the wife's act of divorce via 'Ibanah' matches the husband's intention, except she added a description of immediacy which is disregarded; the original divorce is established. The example given is if she said 'Tallaqtuhu taliqa ba'inah' but it should be a revocable divorce because the husband's delegation is at the level of command, not specification. In contrast, 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of divorce; even if the husband says 'Ikhtartuki' or 'Iktari' intending divorce, it does not effect divorce, and if the wife says 'Ikhtartu nafsi' initially and the husband approves, nothing occurs either. However, an exception is noted: if the husband gives a true choice (takhyir) and she responds with 'Ikhtartu nafsi,' then divorce occurs by consensus of the Companions (ijma). But 'Tilliqi nafsaki' is not considered takhyir, so it is disregarded. The passage also presents a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that even 'Abantu nafsi' does not effect divorce in this context, because 'Ibanah' is different from divorce according to his view. This intra-Hanafi dispute illustrates that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that 'Abantu nafsi' results in a revocable divorce.

Key Terms
ابنت (abantu): This form means 'I have separated/made myself separate'. It is a verbal noun used by the wife to indicate termination of marriage.
اخترت (ikhtartu): I have chosen. Used by wife to indicate exercise of option (takhyir).
تملیک (tamlik): Making the wife owner of the divorce right, as in 'tilliqi nafsaki' where the husband conveys ownership of the divorce act to her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Tilliqi nafsaki' and the wife responds 'Ikhtartu nafsi', what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) A revocable divorce occurs
B) An irrevocable divorce occurs
C) No divorce occurs
D) The divorce is deferred

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce, only responses using recognized divorce words like 'ibanah' effect divorce; 'ikhtiyar' does not unless in true takhyir.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Binding Nature of 'Tilliqi Nafsaki': Tamlik vs Tawkil
Ruling that husband cannot revoke 'tilliqi nafsaki' and it is confined to the session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and tawkil in talaq delegation and state the rulings on revocability and session confinement.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'divorce yourself,' it is a binding tamlik like an oath — he cannot revoke and it expires if she leaves the session.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a key distinction in the delegation of divorce. The husband's choice of words determines the legal nature of the act — whether it is an irrevocable transfer of the right to divorce (tamlik) or a revocable agency (tawkil). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of talaq delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Ruling
What is the legal effect when a husband says 'tilliqi nafsaki'? The passage states he cannot revoke it; it is binding and expires if the wife leaves the session.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the meaning of yamin (oath): the husband suspends divorce on the wife's action, making it a binding disposition.
Yamin is a binding commitment (lazim).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? Because an oath is not revocable, so the delegation has the same binding nature.
The analogy is based on the underlying meaning (ma'na) of the statement.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Counter-Case)
If the husband says 'tilliqi darataka' (divorce your co-wife), it is tawkil, which is revocable and not session-bound.
This distinction is critical for practice.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Practical difference: For 'tilliqi nafsaki', attempts to revoke are ineffective; for 'tilliqi darataka', revocation is valid until executed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ان قال طلقي نفسك فليس له ان يرجع عنه لان فيه معنى اليمين لانه تعليق الطلاق بتطليقها واليمين تصرف لازم ولوقامت عن مجلسها بطل لانه تملیک بخلاف ما اذا قال لها طلقی ضرتک لانه توکیل و انابة فلا يقتصر على المجلس ويقبل الرجوع
And if he said: 'Divorce yourself,' then he does not have the right to revoke it, because it contains the meaning of an oath (yamin), for it is a suspension of divorce upon her divorcing, and an oath is a binding disposition. And if she stood up from her session, it becomes void, because it is a tamlik (making her owner). Contrary to if he said to her: 'Divorce your co-wife,' because that is tawkil (appointment as agent) and deputization, so it is not confined to the session and accepts revocation.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage explains the legal nature of the husband's statement 'Tilliqi nafsaki'. The ruling is that the husband cannot revoke or take back this statement once uttered. The reason is that it carries the meaning of a yamin (oath), because the husband is suspending the occurrence of divorce upon the wife's action of divorcing herself. An oath is a binding disposition; once made, it cannot be undone. Additionally, if the wife stands up and leaves the session (majlis) before acting on it, the delegation becomes void. This is because 'tilliqi nafsaki' is a form of tamlik (making the wife the owner of the right to divorce), and ownership transactions are confined to the session. In contrast, if the husband says 'Tilliqi darataka' (divorce your co-wife), this is considered tawkil (appointment as an agent) and deputization (inaba). Agency is not confined to the session; the wife can exercise it later. Furthermore, the husband can revoke an agency before it is executed. So the distinction between the two cases hinges on whether the husband is delegating his own divorce right (tamlik) or appointing an agent to divorce another (tawkil).

Key Terms
یمین (yamin): An oath; in this context, it means that the statement 'tilliqi nafsaki' is like an oath because it suspends divorce on the wife's action, making it binding and irrevocable.
تملیک (tamlik): Making someone the owner of a right; here, the husband makes the wife the owner of the right to divorce herself, which is a disposition that must be exercised in the session.
توکیل (tawkil): Appointment of an agent; when husband says 'tilliqi darataka', he appoints the wife as agent to divorce the co-wife, which is revocable and not session-bound.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Tilliqi nafsaki' and then immediately says 'I revoke it', what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The revocation is valid and the delegation is cancelled
B) The revocation is invalid and the delegation stands
C) The wife must agree to the revocation
D) The revocation is valid only if the wife has not acted

Key Takeaway
The husband cannot revoke 'tilliqi nafsaki' because it is a binding tamlik, unlike 'tilliqi darataka' which is revocable tawkil.

Matching Response in Delegation of Talaq
Ruling on wife's response in talaq delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when a wife adds a description or uses a different word in response to her husband's delegation of talaq and identify whether divorce occurs.

Core Idea
In delegation of talaq, the wife's response must match the husband's wording exactly; extra descriptions are ignored, and different words do not effect divorce.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on what happens when a husband says 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) and the wife responds with additional description or a different expression. The principle is strict: the wife's response must conform to the husband's delegation for the divorce to take effect as delegated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation wording
Recognize that the husband's phrase 'talliqi nafsak' is a clear delegation of the power to divorce. The wife's response must be in words that are synonymous with talaq to be valid.
2
Compare the wife's response
Examine whether the wife's words exactly match the husband's delegation. If she adds a description like 'bā'inah' (irrevocable), the divorce is still valid as raj'i (revocable) and the description is void.
Addition of description does not invalidate the divorce.
3
Determine if the words are talaq words
If the wife uses a word that is not among the explicit words of talaq, such as 'ikhtīār' (choice) or 'abāna' (separation), no divorce occurs because the response does not match the husband's delegation.
The word 'ikhtīār' is only considered talaq in the context of takhyīr (giving choice), not here.
4
Apply the ruling to the specific case
Based on the above, decide: if the wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi tatlīqatan bā'inah', the divorce is valid as raj'i. If she says 'ikhtartu nafsī' or 'abantu nafsī', no divorce occurs.
Imam Abu Hanifa's view on 'abāna' is that it does not effect divorce.

He said: 'Divorce yourself.' The wife agreed but added a description of ba'ina (irrevocability) in her response. So the original divorce is established, and the extra description is void. This is like if in response to 'talliqi nafsak' the wife said 'tallaqtu nafsi tatlīqatan bā'inah'. Contrary to the word 'ikhtīār' because the word 'ikhtīār' is not among the words of divorce. Thus, if a man says to his wife with intention of divorce 'ikhtārtī' or 'ikhtisārī', or the wife initiates saying 'ikhtartu nafsī' and the man responds with 'ajaztu', in these three cases divorce does not occur. The word 'ikhtīār' being divorce, contrary to qiyās, is established by consensus of the Companions only when it occurs in response to takhyīr (giving choice), i.e., the man says 'ikhṭārī' and the wife responds 'ikhtartu nafsī'. And here, since the husband's saying 'talliqi nafsak' is not takhyīr, the wife's saying 'ikhtartu nafsī' is void because it does not match the man's saying 'talliqi nafsak'. It is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that if in response to the man's saying 'talliqi nafsak' the wife says 'abantu nafsī', then also divorce does not occur because in this case too the wife's words do not match the man's words, because the man has delegated divorce and the wife has effected abāna (separation), and abāna is different from divorce.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary. It establishes that when a husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself), the wife's response must match his wording exactly for the divorce to take effect as delegated. If she adds a description like 'bā'inah' (irrevocable), the divorce is valid as raj'i (revocable) and the extra description is ignored. However, if she uses a different word that is not among the explicit words of divorce, such as 'ikhtartu nafsī' (I choose myself) or 'abantu nafsī' (I separate myself), then no divorce occurs because the response does not match the husband's delegation. The passage also explains that the word 'ikhtīār' (choice) is only considered a divorce word by consensus of the Companions in the specific context of takhyīr (giving choice), which does not apply here. A narration from Imam Abu Hanifa confirms that 'abantu nafsī' also does not result in divorce because 'abāna' is not the same as talaq.

Key Terms
بینونت (baynūnāt): Irrevocability; a description added by wife meaning talaq ba'in (irrevocable divorce).
اختیار (ikhtīār): Choice; a word used in delegation where husband gives wife choice, considered a word of divorce by consensus in that context.
ابانت (abānat): Separation; a word used by wife meaning she declares herself separated, not considered a word of talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because she added an extra description.
B) A valid talaq raj'i occurs and the description is ignored.
C) A valid talaq ba'in occurs as she stated.
D) The husband must give a new talaq.

Key Takeaway
In delegation of talaq, the wife's response must exactly match the husband's wording; any extra description is void, and using a non-talaq word prevents divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Tamlik vs Tawkil in Delegation of Divorce
Ruling that delegation to wife is tamlik, non-revocable, and limited to session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and tawkil in talaq delegation and apply the ruling on revocability and session limitation.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'divorce yourself,' it is an irrevocable tamlik; when he says 'divorce your co-wife,' it is a revocable tawkil.

The Hanafi school draws a sharp distinction between two types of delegation in divorce: when a husband tells his wife to divorce herself, he makes her the owner of the decision—this is tamlik and is binding. But when he tells her to divorce her co-wife, he appoints her as his agent—tawkil—which remains revocable. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this distinction and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two statements: 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) and 'talliqi daratik' (divorce your co-wife). Note the different rulings on revocability and session limitation.
2
Analyze the nature of each delegation
Determine why 'talliqi nafsak' is tamlik and 'talliqi daratik' is tawkil. The key is whether the wife acts for herself (tamlik) or for the husband (tawkil).
This distinction is based on the capacity of the wife to own her own divorce but not her co-wife's.
3
Understand the role of yamin (oath)
'Talliqi nafsak' contains the meaning of an oath (yamin) because it suspends divorce on the wife's action, making the husband's statement binding. In contrast, 'talliqi daratik' does not involve a genuine oath because the co-wife's divorce is certain if delegated.
Certainty eliminates the ta'liq (suspension) element, so no yamin.
4
Apply the rulings to practical cases
Use the derived rule: tamlik is non-revocable and expires with the session; tawkil is revocable and extends beyond the session.
For example, if the wife leaves the room after 'talliqi nafsak', she loses her option; after 'talliqi daratik', she retains it.

And if he says: 'Divorce yourself,' then he does not have the right to revoke it, because it contains the meaning of an oath (yamin), since it is suspending divorce upon her divorcing, and an oath is a binding disposition. And if she leaves her session, it becomes void because it is tamlik (ownership). Contrary to when he says to her: 'Divorce your co-wife,' because it is tawkil (delegation) and deputizing, so it is not confined to the session and accepts revocation. Translation: And if the man said: 'Divorce yourself,' then the husband does not have the choice to revoke his statement. Because in delegation there is the meaning of yamin (suspension). This is because it is suspending divorce upon the wife divorcing. And yamin is a binding disposition. So he does not have the right to revoke. And if the wife stood up from her session, her option becomes void, because through 'talliqi nafsak' the wife is made owner of divorce, and ownerships are dependent on the session. So standing from the session voids the wife's option. Contrary to if the husband said: 'Divorce your co-wife,' because this makes the wife an agent and deputy for divorcing her co-wife, and agency is not dependent on the session and accepts revocation. Therefore, in this case the husband can revoke his statement. Summary: This issue has two rulings: one is necessity (luzūm) considering the meaning of yamin; the second is dependence on the session considering the meaning of tamlik. There are two objections here. First: why is 'talliqi nafsak' specified with yamin and not 'talliqi daratik', while just as 'talliqi nafsak' means 'if you divorce yourself then you are divorced', similarly 'talliqi daratik' means 'if you intend/desire her divorce then she is divorced'? What is the reason for the difference? Second: what is the reason for specifying the first as tamlik and the second as tawkil? Answer to first objection: The meaning of yamin is realized by suspending something whose existence is uncertain; divorce of the co-wife, if delegated to the wife, its existence is certain habitually and naturally, so it does not qualify as a condition. Answer to second objection: It has passed earlier that the owner (mālik) acts for himself, and the agent (wakīl) acts for someone else. The wife acts for herself in her own divorce, and for her husband in the divorce of her co-wife. Thus, the wife can be owner of her own divorce but not of her co-wife's divorce.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary. It establishes that when a husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself), his statement becomes binding (lāzim) like an oath, and he cannot revoke it. The wife's right to divorce herself is limited to the session (majlis) because it is tamlik (making her owner of the divorce). In contrast, if he says 'talliqi daratik' (divorce your co-wife), that is tawkil (agency), so the husband can revoke it, and the wife's authority is not limited to the session. The text addresses two objections: (1) Why is 'talliqi nafsak' treated as an oath while 'talliqi daratik' is not, given both are conditional? The answer is that the co-wife's divorce is certain if delegated, so suspension (ta'liq) is not meaningful. (2) Why is the first tamlik and the second tawkil? The answer is that the wife acts for herself in her own divorce (so she is owner) and for her husband in the co-wife's divorce (so she is agent). This distinction is critical in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
تملیک (tamlik): Making the wife owner of the divorce; it is binding and confined to the session.
توکیل (tawkil): Agency; making someone a deputy to act on one's behalf; here the wife becomes agent for divorcing the co-wife.
یمین (yamin): Oath; the husband's statement is treated like an oath because it suspends divorce on a condition, making it binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a form of tawkil (agency).
B) Because it contains the meaning of yamin (oath), which is a binding disposition.
C) Because the wife's acceptance is required for the delegation to be effective.
D) Because the husband cannot delegate talaq to his wife.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between tamlik (ownership) and tawkil (agency) in divorce delegation: 'talliqi nafsak' is tamlik (binding, session-limited), while 'talliqi daratik' is tawkil (revocable, not session-limited).

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Delegation in Divorce: Tawkil vs Tamlik
Rulings on unlimited delegation and delegation to a third party with condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tawkil and tamlik in divorce delegation and apply the Hanafi ruling on delegation with the condition 'in shi'ta'.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'if you wish' to a third man, divorce becomes tamlik — binding and session-limited.

This passage examines three scenarios of delegation in talaq. It clarifies how the wording changes the legal nature from agency (tawkil) to ownership (tamlik), affecting revocability and time limits. The key is the effect of the conditional phrase 'in shi'ta' (if you wish). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly executing delegated divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three delegation scenarios
List the three cases: (1) husband tells wife 'divorce yourself whenever you wish', (2) husband tells a man 'divorce my wife', (3) husband tells a man 'divorce her if you wish'.
Each scenario has a distinct ruling in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Note the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1: wife can divorce anytime (in or after session). For scenario 2: man can divorce anytime, husband can revoke (tawkil). For scenario 3: man can only divorce in session, husband cannot revoke (tamlik).
The time limit and revocability differentiate tawkil from tamlik.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Why is scenario 1 different from scenario 2? Because the wife acts for herself, it is tamlik. Why is scenario 3 different from scenario 2? Because the addition of 'in shi'ta' makes it tamlik: the man becomes owner of the action, so it is binding and limited to the session.
The word 'mata' (whenever) gives perpetual authority; the word 'in shi'ta' (if you wish) transfers ownership.
4
Analyze Imam Zufar's objection
Imam Zufar equated scenario 3 with scenario 2, arguing that mentioning 'if you wish' is superfluous since the man always acts by his own wish. He analogized it to a wakil for sale told 'sell if you wish'.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between divorce and sale regarding tolerance of ta'liq (suspension).
5
Apply the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is that divorce can tolerate suspension (ta'liq), so the condition 'in shi'ta' is meaningful and creates tamlik. Sale does not tolerate suspension, so the analogy fails.
This distinction is a key usul principle: talaq can be conditioned, but sale cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال لها طلقى نفسك متى شئت فلها ان تطلق نفسها فى المجلس وبعده لان كلمة متى عامة في الاوقات كلها فصار كما اذا قال فى اى وقت شئت واذا قال لرجل طلق امرأتى فله ان يطلقها في المجلس وبعده وله ان يرجع لانه توكيل وانه استعانة فلا يلزم ولا يقتصر على المجلس بخلاف قوله لامرأته طلقی نفسك لانها عاملة لنفسها فكان تمليكا لاتوكيلا ولوقال لرجل طلقها ان شئت فله ان يطلقها في المجلس خاصة وليس للزوج ان يرجع وقال زفر رحمه الله هذا والاول سواء لان التصريح بالمشية كعدمه لانه يتصرف عن مشية فصار كالوكيل بالبيع اذا قيل له بع ان شئت ولنا انه تملیک لانه علقه بالمشية والمالك هو الذى يتصرف عن مشية والاطلاق يحتمل التعليق بخلاف البيع لانه لا يحتمله
ترجمہ اور اگر عورت سے کہا کہ تو اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے جس وقت تو چاہے تو عورت کو اختیار ہے کہ اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے مجلس میں اور مجلس کے بعد ۔ کیونکہ کلمہ منی تمام اوقات میں عام ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب کہانی ای وقت شئت ۔ اور جب کہا کسی مرد سے میری بیوی کو طلاق دے دے تو اس کیلئے اختیار ہے کہ وہ اس کو مجلس اور مجلس کے بعد طلاق دے۔ اور (شوہر) کیلئے جائز ہے کہ وہ رجوع کرے۔ کیونکہ یہ تو کیل اور استعانت ہے۔ لہذا نہ لازم ہوگا اور نہ مجلس پر منحصر رہے گا۔ بخلاف اس کے کہ (اس نے ) اپنی بیوی سے کہا تو اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنے واسطے عمل کرنے والی ہے۔ پس یہ تملیک ہوگی نہ کہ تو کیل۔ اور اگر مرد نے کسی دوسرے مرد سے کہا کہ تو اسکو ( میری بیوی کو طلاق دے دے اگر تو چاہے تو اس کیلئے جائز ہے کہ وہ اس کو خاص طور سے مجلس میں طلاق دے دئے اور شوہر کو رجوع کرنے کا بھی اختیار نہیں ہے۔ اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ یہ اور اول دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیونکہ مشیت کی صراحت عدم صراحت کے مانند ہے۔ اسلئے کہ وہ اپنی مشیت ہی سے تصرف کرتا ہے۔ پس وکیل بالبیع کے مانند ہو گیا جبکہ اس سے کہا جائے اگر تو چاہے تو اس کو فروخت کرے۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ تملیک ہے کیونکہ اسکو مشیت پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور مالک وہی ہے جو اپنی مشیت سے تصرف کرتا ہے ۔اور طلاق تعلیق کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ برخلاف بیچ کے، کیونکہ وہ تعلیق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی۔
And if he says to her: 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish,' then she may divorce herself in the session and after it, because the word 'mata' is general for all times, so it becomes as if he said 'in any time you wish.' And if he says to a man: 'Divorce my wife,' then he may divorce her in the session and after it, and the husband may revoke, because it is tawkil (agency) and seeking help, so it is not binding and not confined to the session. Contrary to his saying to his wife 'talliqi nafsak' because she acts for herself, so it is tamlik not tawkil. And if he said to a man: 'Divorce her if you wish,' then he may divorce her specifically in the session, and the husband does not have the right to revoke. Imam Zufar said: this and the first are equal, because explicit mention of wish is like its absence, since he acts by his wish, so it becomes like a wakil for sale when told 'sell if you wish.' Our proof is that this is tamlik because he suspended it on wish, and the owner is the one who acts by his wish, and divorce can tolerate suspension, contrary to sale because it does not tolerate it. Translation: And if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish,' then the wife has the option to divorce herself in the session and after it, because the word 'mata' is general for all times, so it becomes as if he said 'in any time you wish.' And if he said to a man: 'Divorce my wife,' then he has the option to divorce her in the session and after it, and the husband may revoke, because this is tawkil and seeking help, so it is not binding and not confined to the session. Contrary to his saying to his wife 'talliqi nafsak' because she acts for herself, so it is tamlik not tawkil. And if a man said to another man: 'Divorce her if you wish,' then he may divorce her specifically in the session, and the husband does not have the right to revoke. Imam Zufar said: this and the first are equal, because explicit mention of wish is like its absence, since he acts by his wish, so it becomes like a wakil for sale when told 'sell if you wish.' Our proof is that this is tamlik because he suspended it on wish, and the owner is the one who acts by his wish, and divorce can tolerate suspension, contrary to sale because it does not tolerate it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering three scenarios. First, if the husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsak mata shi'ti' (divorce yourself whenever you wish), the wife can divorce herself in or after the session; the word 'mata' is general for all times, so she has perpetual authority. Second, if the husband tells a third man 'talliq imra'ati' (divorce my wife), this is tawkil (agency) and isti'ana (seeking help), so it is not binding, the husband can revoke, and the man can divorce in or after the session. Third, if the husband tells a man 'talliqha in shi'ta' (divorce her if you wish), then the man can only divorce in the session, and the husband cannot revoke. Imam Zufar disagreed with this third ruling, arguing that adding 'in shi'ta' is meaningless since the man always acts by his wish; thus it should be like the second scenario. The Hanafi response is that this is tamlik (ownership) because it is suspended on the man's wish, and the owner acts by his wish. Divorce can tolerate suspension (ta'liq) while sale cannot, so the analogy with sale is invalid. This distinction is important in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
متی (mata): Whenever; a general time word indicating all times, giving wife perpetual authority to divorce.
تملیک (tamlik): Making the person owner of the divorce; here the third man becomes owner of the act, so it is binding and session-limited.
تعلیق (ta'liq): Suspension; attaching a ruling to a condition. Divorce can be suspended, but sale cannot in Hanafi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between telling a man 'talliq imra'ati' and 'talliqha in shi'ta'?
A) In the first, the man can only divorce in the session; in the second, anytime.
B) In the first, the husband can revoke; in the second, he cannot.
C) In the first, it is tamlik; in the second, it is tawkil.
D) There is no difference according to Imam Zufar, but the Hanafi school says there is.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between tawkil and tamlik in delegation turns on whether the action is conditional on the delegate's wish; tamlik is binding and session-limited.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Wife may divorce herself at any time when told 'taliqi nafsaki mata shi'ta'
Ruling on delegation of divorce with indefinite time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that when a husband says 'taliqi nafsaki mata shi'ta', the wife may divorce at any time, and explain the linguistic basis for the generality.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'whenever you wish', the wife's option is unlimited in time because 'mata' is general.

In the Hanafi school, the wording used by a husband when delegating divorce to his wife determines the scope of her authority. This lesson examines the phrase 'taliqi nafsaki mata shi'ta' — 'divorce yourself whenever you wish' — and why it gives the wife an ongoing right to divorce, not limited to the session. The key lies in the linguistic analysis of the word 'mata' (whenever).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State what act is being ruled upon: the husband tells his wife 'taliqi nafsaki mata shi'ta'. Determine the hukm: the wife's divorce is valid whether she acts in the session or later.
The ruling is that the divorce is effective immediately upon her pronouncement.
2
Locate the dalil
Identify the proof cited: the linguistic principle that the word 'mata' is 'amm (general) in time.
The dalil is aqli (rational linguistic analysis) rather than a specific text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling: because 'mata' indicates generality, the wife's right extends to all times, just as if the husband had said 'at any time you wish'.
The generality is derived from the particle itself, not from external context.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may restrict the option to the session. The Hanafi school differs and allows it after, based on the unrestricted nature of 'mata'.
This ikhtilaf affects the practical outcome of when the wife can act.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that the wife may divorce at any time, as stated in the zahir al-riwayah.
This is the position presented by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah without dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلقی نفسک متی شئت کہا تو عورت مجلس اور مجلس کے بعد اپنے آپ کو طلاق دے سکتی ہے وان قال لها طلقى نفسك متى شئت فلها ان تطلق نفسها فى المجلس وبعده لان كلمة متى عامة في الاوقات كلها فصار كما اذا قال فى اى وقت شئت ترجمہ اور اگر عورت سے کہا کہ تو اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے جس وقت تو چاہے تو عورت کو اختیار ہے کہ اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے مجلس میں اور مجلس کے بعد ۔ کیونکہ کلمہ متی تمام اوقات میں عام ہے۔ پس ایسا ہو گیا جیسا کہ جب کہا فی ای وقت شئت ۔
He said to her: 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish.' She has the right to divorce herself in the session and after it, because the word 'mata' is general for all times, so it becomes as if he said 'at any time you wish.'
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This is a ruling from the book of divorce regarding delegation (tafwid). The husband says to his wife 'taliqi nafsaki mata shi'ta' meaning 'divorce yourself whenever you wish.' This gives the wife the authority to effect the divorce at any time, whether in the same gathering or later. The proof is linguistic: the word 'mata' (whenever) is a general term covering all times. Therefore, the wife's option is not restricted to the session. This is classified as tamlik (transfer of ownership of the right to divorce) because the wife acts for herself. The ruling applies as long as the husband does not revoke, but in tamlik, revocation is not allowed as will be explained.

Key Terms
متى (mata): A conditional word meaning 'whenever', used to indicate generality in time.
مجلس (majlis): The session or gathering in which the husband gives the statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only after the session
C) During or after the session
D) Only with the husband's permission

Key Takeaway
When a husband delegates divorce using general time words like 'mata shi'ta', the wife retains the right to divorce at any time, based on the linguistic generality of 'mata'.

Talaq — Divorce

Tawkil vs Tamlik in Divorce: Agency and Transfer of Right
Compares two delegation types in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tawkil (agency) and tamlik (transfer) in divorce, and state the legal consequences of each.

Core Idea
Delegating divorce to a third party is revocable agency; delegating to the wife is a binding transfer of right.

This lesson clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi divorce law. When a husband asks another man to divorce his wife, that is tawkil (agency) — the husband can revoke and the agent can act later. But when he tells his wife 'divorce yourself,' that is tamlik — the wife owns the right and it is limited to the session. Understanding the difference prevents common errors in marriage and divorce practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two delegations
Read the passage and note the two scenarios: the husband saying to a man 'taliq imra'ati' and to his wife 'taliqi nafsaki'.
These are two distinct legal acts with different rulings.
2
Determine the legal nature of each
Classify the first as tawkil (agency) because the man acts on behalf of the husband. Classify the second as tamlik (transfer) because the wife acts for herself.
The key is who is acting for whom.
3
Extract the consequences of tawkil
From the passage: the agent can divorce in the session or later, and the husband can revoke. This is because tawkil is a non-binding assistance (isti'anah).
Compare with tamlik: the wife's option is restricted to the session and the husband cannot revoke.
4
Contrast with tamlik
In tamlik, the wife is acting for herself, so the right is transferred to her. Hence it is binding and time-limited.
This distinction is well-established in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a new scenario, determine whether it is tawkil or tamlik and state the corresponding ruling on revocability and time limit.
Practice with examples to solidify understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال لرجل طلق امرأتى فله ان يطلقها في المجلس وبعده وله ان يرجع لانه توكيل وانه استعانة فلا يلزم ولا يقتصر على المجلس بخلاف قوله لامرأته طلقی نفسك لانها عاملة لنفسها فكان تمليكا لاتوكيلا ترجمہ اور جب کہا کسی مرد سے میری بیوی کو طلاق دے دے تو اس کیلئے اختیار ہے کہ وہ اس کو مجلس اور مجلس کے بعد طلاق دے۔ اور (شوہر) کیلئے جائز ہے کہ وہ رجوع کرے۔ کیونکہ یہ تو کیل اور استعانت ہے۔ لہذا نہ لازم ہوگا اور نہ مجلس پر منحصر رہے گا۔ بخلاف اس کے کہ (اس نے ) اپنی بیوی سے کہا تو اپنے نفس کو طلاق دے۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنے واسطے عمل کرنے والی ہے۔ پس یہ تملیک ہوگی نہ کہ تو کیل۔
And if he said to a man 'Divorce my wife,' he has the right to divorce her in the session and after it, and he (the husband) has the right to revoke because it is a tawkil (agency) and a request for assistance, so it is not binding and is not restricted to the session, unlike when he says to his wife 'Divorce yourself' because she acts for herself, so it is tamlik, not tawkil.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two types of delegation in divorce. When a husband tells another man 'taliq imra'ati' (divorce my wife), this is tawkil (agency). The agent (the man) is acting on behalf of the husband. Therefore, the agent can divorce the wife in the session or later, and the husband can revoke the agency at any time. In contrast, when the husband tells his wife 'taliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself), this is tamlik (transfer of ownership of the right to divorce). The wife acts for herself, so it is not agency but rather a gift of the right. Consequently, the wife's option is restricted to the session (as per other rulings), and the husband cannot revoke because he has transferred ownership.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; the husband appoints a third person to effect the divorce on his behalf. The agent acts for the husband.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership of the right to divorce to the wife. She acts for herself, not as an agent.
استعانة (isti'anah): Seeking assistance. The husband seeks the third person's help in effecting the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband tells his wife: 'Divorce yourself if you want.' Which of the following is true about this delegation?
A) The wife can divorce at any future time.
B) The husband can revoke the delegation at any time.
C) The wife must divorce immediately in that session only.
D) The husband's permission is needed for the divorce to be valid.

Key Takeaway
Delegating divorce to a third party is a revocable agency (tawkil), while delegating to the wife is a binding transfer (tamlik) limited to the session.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Delegation of Divorce with 'In Shi'ta': Tamlik vs Tawkil
Presents ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Zufr regarding delegation with condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between tamlik and tawkil in delegated divorce and evaluate Zufr's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Adding 'in shi'ta' transforms delegation into tamlik, restricting the recipient's authority to the session.

This passage examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Imam Zufr regarding the effect of the condition 'in shi'ta' (if you wish) when delegating divorce to a third party. Understanding the distinction between tamlik and tawkil is crucial for determining the validity and limits of the delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The husband says to a third man: 'Taliqha in shi'ta' (Divorce her if you wish). Note that the condition 'in shi'ta' is explicitly attached.
This is not an unconditional delegation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that this is tamlik (transfer of ownership of the right to divorce). The man can only divorce in that session, and the husband cannot revoke.
The condition makes the recipient the 'owner' of the right for that session.
3
State Zufr's opposing position
Zufr argues it is tawkil (agency) just like unconditional delegation, because mentioning the wish is redundant since an agent always acts by his own wish. He analogizes to an agent for sale told 'Sell if you wish'.
Zufr views the condition as legally insignificant.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that divorce can be suspended (ta'liq) while sale cannot. Therefore, the analogy to sale is invalid (qiyas ma'a al-fariq). The condition 'in shi'ta' actually indicates tamlik because the recipient acts on his own ownership.
The key distinction: ta'liq is possible in talaq but not in sale.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
If tamlik: man's authority limited to session; husband cannot revoke. If tawkil: man can divorce anytime; husband can revoke. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is mufta bihi.
The disagreement produces different legal outcomes in real cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال لرجل طلقها ان شئت فله ان يطلقها في المجلس خاصة وليس للزوج ان يرجع وقال زفر رحمه الله هذا والاول سواء لان التصريح بالمشية كعدمه لانه يتصرف عن مشية فصار كالوكيل بالبيع اذا قيل له بع ان شئت ولنا انه تملیک لانه علقه بالمشية والمالك هو الذى يتصرف عن مشية والاطلاق يحتمل التعليق بخلاف البيع لانه لا يحتمله ترجمہ اور اگر مرد نے کسی دوسرے مرد سے کہا کہ تو اسکو ( میری بیوی کو طلاق دے دے اگر تو چاہے تو اس کیلئے جائز ہے کہ وہ اس کو خاص طور سے مجلس میں طلاق دے دئے اور شوہر کو رجوع کرنے کا بھی اختیار نہیں ہے۔ اور امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ یہ اور اول دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیونکہ مشیت کی صراحت عدم صراحت کے مانند ہے۔ اسلئے کہ وہ اپنی مشیت ہی سے تصرف کرتا ہے۔ پس وکیل بالبیع کے مانند ہو گیا جبکہ اس سے کہا جائے اگر تو چاہے تو اس کو فروخت کرے۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ تملیک ہے کیونکہ اسکو مشیت پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور مالک وہی ہے جو اپنی مشیت سے تصرف کرتا ہے ۔اور طلاق تعلیق کا احتمال رکھتی ہے۔ برخلاف بیچ کے، کیونکہ وہ تعلیق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی۔ والطلاق يحتمل التعليق سے امام زفر کے قیاس کا جواب ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ طلاق کو بیچ پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ اسلئے کہ طلاق تعلیق کا احتمال رکھتی ہے اور بیچ تعلیق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ۔
And if he said to a man 'Divorce her if you wish,' he has the right to divorce her only in the session, and the husband does not have the right to revoke. Zufr said: This and the first are equal because the mention of the condition is like not mentioning it, because he acts by his own wish, so it becomes like an agent for sale when it is said to him 'Sell if you wish.' And for us, it is tamlik because he suspended it on the condition, and the owner is the one who acts by his own wish, and divorce can be suspended, unlike sale because it cannot be suspended.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where the husband says to a third party 'Taliqha in shi'ta' (divorce her if you wish). According to Abu Hanifa, this is tamlik (transfer of ownership) because the act is conditioned on the man's wish, indicating ownership. Therefore, the man's authority is restricted to the session, and the husband cannot revoke. Imam Zufr argues that it is tawkil (agency) because mentioning the condition is redundant since the agent already acts by his own wish. He analogizes it to an agent for sale who is told 'Sell if you wish' where the agent is not restricted. The Hanafi response is that divorce (talaq) can be suspended (ta'liq) while sale cannot, making the analogy invalid. Hence, the ruling stands as tamlik.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership of the right of divorce; the recipient acts for himself.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; the person acts on behalf of the husband.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of the effect of a contract on a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband tells a man 'Taliqha in shi'ta' (divorce her if you wish), when can the man divorce her?
A) Any time
B) Only in the session
C) Only after the session
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
Adding 'in shi'ta' to a delegation of divorce changes it from tawkil to tamlik, restricting the recipient's authority to the session and barring the husband's revocation.

Wife gives herself one divorce when granted three: ownership includes the lesser
States ruling and reasoning for tamlik with partial exercise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife exercises fewer divorces than granted under tamlik, and explain the legal reasoning using the principle that the greater includes the lesser.

Core Idea
Ownership of three divorces includes ownership of one by necessity.

This passage clarifies a simple but important point in the law of tamlik (delegated divorce). When a husband grants his wife the right to pronounce up to three divorces, and she pronounces only one, the effect is a single divorce. The reasoning is straightforward: the greater power encompasses the lesser.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's act and the wife's response
The husband says 'Taliqi nafsaki thalatha' (divorce yourself three times). The wife divorces once. Note that the wife has not used the full number granted.
2
Determine the legal effect of the wife's action
The ruling is that a single divorce occurs. It does not matter that she was granted three; she may use any number up to the maximum.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is rational: ownership of three necessarily includes ownership of one. If she has the power to effect three divorces, she certainly has the power to effect one. This is a logical consequence of the grant.
4
Note the consensus
This ruling is settled within the Hanafi school without dispute. There is no intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, and it is consistent with the principle of tamlik.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو قال لها طلقى نفسك ثلثا فطلقت واحدة فهى واحدة لانها ملكت ايقاع الثلث فتملك ايقاع الواحد ضرورة ترجمہ اور اگر (کسی مرد) نے عورت سے کہا تو اپنے نفس کو تین طلاقیں دے۔ پس اس نے ایک طلاق واقع کی ۔ تو یہ ایک ہوگی۔ کیونکہ وہ عورت تین کو واقع کرنے کی مالک ہو گئی۔ لہذا ایک کو واقع کرنے کی بھی بداہتہ مالک ہوگی۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو تین طلاق واقع کرنے کا اختیار دیا۔ بیوی نے اپنے اوپر ایک واقع کی ۔ تو یہ ایک طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب عورت تین طلاقوں کی مالک ہے تو ان کے ضمن میں ایک کی بھی مالک ہوگی۔
And if he said to her 'Divorce yourself three times,' and she divorced once, it is one because she owned the power to effect three, so she owns the power to effect one by necessity.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This is a simple ruling regarding tamlik. When the husband gives the wife the option to divorce herself three times, she has been granted ownership (tamlik) of up to three divorces. If she exercises only one, it is valid and counts as one divorce. The reasoning is that the greater includes the lesser; since she owns the right to three, she necessarily owns the right to one. This is a straightforward application of the principle of tamlik.

Key Terms
مالك (malik): Owner; she has ownership of the right to divorce.

D) The marriage becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
When a wife is given the right to three divorces, she may validly use any number up to three; using one counts as one divorce.

Wakalah vs Tamlik in Delegating Divorce
Explanation of two forms of divorce delegation with legal consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wakalah and tamlik in delegating divorce and state the respective rulings on timing and revocability.

Core Idea
When delegating divorce, saying 'divorce my wife' makes an agent; saying 'divorce yourself' grants ownership—different rules apply.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hadaya clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi divorce law: delegating divorce to another man is agency (wakalah), while delegating to the wife is granting ownership (tamlik). Though both involve another person effecting the divorce, the legal consequences differ in two critical ways: the time limit for exercise and the husband's right to revoke. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rules of talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: husband says to another man 'talliq imra'ati' (divorce my wife) and husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsik' (divorce yourself). Note the Arabic phrases.
Phrases are distinct and lead to different rulings.
2
Classify each as wakalah or tamlik
Determine which phrase creates agency and which creates ownership of the divorce right. 'Talliq imra'ati' is wakalah; 'talliqi nafsik' is tamlik.
The classification is based on the nature of the delegation.
3
Extract the ruling for wakalah
For agency, the agent can divorce in the session or after, and the husband can revoke the agency at any time before divorce.
Wakalah is not binding and not restricted to the session.
4
Extract the ruling for tamlik
For ownership, the wife can only divorce in the session of the utterance, and the husband cannot revoke his grant.
Tamlik is binding and restricted to the session.
5
Understand the underlying reason (talil)
Why does wakalah allow more flexibility? Because the agent acts for another, so the principal retains control. Why does tamlik restrict to session and bar revocation? Because the wife acts for herself, so it is like a gift that becomes immediately effective.
The key is whether the person acts for himself (tamlik) or for another (wakalah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کہ ایک مرد نے دوسرے سے کہا طلق امراتی ۔ تو اس وکیل کیلئے اختیار ہے کہ وہ اس عورت کو مجلس میں طلاق دے یا مجلس کے بعد ۔ اور شوہر کیلئے اپنے قول سے رجوع کرنا بھی جائز ہے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ طلق امرأتی کے ذریعہ شوہر نے دوسرے آدمی کو وکیل بنایا ہے۔ اور طلاق واقع کرنے میں اس سے مدد طلب کی ہے۔ اور تو کیل نہ لازم ہوتی ہے اور نہ مجلس پر منحصر اسلئے وکیل بالطلاق کو اختیار ہوگا کہ وہ مجلس میں طلاق دے یا مجلس کے بعد ۔ اور شوہر کو رجوع کا اختیار ہو گا اس کے برخلاف اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے طلق نفسک کہا، تو عورت کا یہ اختیار مجلس کے ساتھ خاص ہوگا۔ اور شوہر کو اپنے قول سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار بھی حاصل نہیں ہوگا۔ وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ شوہر کا قول طلقی نفسک تملیک ہے۔ کیونکہ عورت اپنے واسطے عمل کرے گی۔ اور وکیل وہ ہے جو اپنے غیر کیلئے عمل کرے۔ اور یہ سابق میں بیان ہو چکا ہے کہ تملیک کی صورت میں شوہر کو رجوع کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا اور مجلس کے ساتھ تملیک خاص ہوتی ہے۔
That a man said to another: 'Divorce my wife.' So that agent has the option to divorce that woman in the session or after the session. And the husband also has the right to revoke his statement. The proof is that by 'divorce my wife' the husband appointed the other man as an agent, and sought his help in effecting divorce. And agency is neither binding nor restricted to the session, therefore the agent for divorce will have the option to divorce in the session or after the session, and the husband will have the option to revoke. In contrast, if the husband said to his wife 'divorce yourself,' then the wife's option is restricted to the session, and the husband does not have the option to revoke his statement. The reason for the difference is that the husband's statement 'divorce yourself' is tamlīk, because the wife acts for herself, while the agent is one who acts for another. And it has been previously stated that in the case of tamlīk, the husband does not have the option to revoke, and tamlīk is restricted to the session.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between two types of delegation of divorce: wakalah (agency) and tamlik (granting ownership). When a husband says to another man 'talliq imra'ati' (divorce my wife), it is wakalah - the man becomes an agent. In agency, the agent is not bound to act nor restricted to the session; he may divorce at any time. The husband may also revoke the agency before divorce is executed. In contrast, when the husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsik' (divorce yourself), it is tamlik - the husband grants the wife ownership of the divorce right. In tamlik, the wife can only divorce in the session of the utterance, and the husband cannot revoke his grant. The reason is that in wakalah, the agent acts for another, so the principal retains control; in tamlik, the wife acts for herself, so it is like a gift which becomes effective immediately and cannot be taken back.

Key Terms
طلق أمرأتي (talliq imra'ati): Phrase used by husband to delegate divorce to another man, establishing wakalah
طلق نفسك (talliqi nafsik): Phrase used by husband to delegate divorce to his wife, establishing tamlik
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; acting on behalf of another
تمليك (tamlik): Granting ownership; giving the right to divorce to the wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wife can divorce at any time.
B) The husband can revoke his statement.
C) The wife must divorce in the session.
D) The wife can delegate to another.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between wakalah and tamlik in talaq delegation determines the timing of the right and the husband's ability to revoke.

Conditional Delegation of Divorce: The Zufar Dispute
Hanafi ruling and Imam Zufar's challenge on conditional divorce delegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for 'talliqha in shi'ta' to a third party, explain why it is tamlik with ta'liq, and critique Imam Zufar's analogy to wakalah in sale.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'divorce her if you wish,' it is tamlik with ta'liq — restricted to the session and irrevocable.

This lesson examines a subtle yet critical dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the phrase 'talliqha in shi'ta' (divorce her if you wish) addressed to a third party. While Imam Zufar treats it as a simple agency (wakalah) akin to sale, the majority Hanafi position distinguishes it as a hybrid of tamlik (conferring ownership of divorce) and ta'liq (suspension), leading to different temporal and revocability rules. Understanding this distinction deepens your grasp of how the Hanafi school classifies delegation of divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and the dissenting view
Identify the majority Hanafi position (tamlik with ta'liq: agent can only divorce in the session, husband cannot revoke) and Imam Zufar's view (pure wakalah: agent can divorce anytime, husband can revoke).
Imam Zufar is a Hanafi scholar, so this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
2
Examine Imam Zufar's proof: qiyas on wakalah in sale
Understand his analogy: 'talliqha in shi'ta' is like 'sell if you wish' to a sales agent. In both, the condition is implicit, so the rules of agency (wakalah) apply: no session restriction and revocability.
He analogizes the lack of explicit mention of wish to the first case 'talliq imra'ati' where the agent acts by his own wish.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: tamlik and ta'liq
The majority argues that 'talliqha in shi'ta' contains two elements: tamlik (like giving the right to the wife herself, restricted to the session) and ta'liq (making the divorce conditional on the agent's wish, making it binding). Thus, it is not pure agency.
Tamlik is proprietary; wakalah is fiduciary. The wording 'in shi'ta' adds a condition that makes the action suspended (mu'allaq) upon wish.
4
Identify the key distinction: divorce can be suspended, sale cannot
The Hanafi response uses the principle 'al-talaq yahamil al-ta'liq' (divorce is open to suspension). Since sale cannot be suspended (a conditional sale is fasid), analogizing divorce to sale is qiyas ma'a al-fariq (analogy with a difference).
This distinction is a core Hanafi usul point: the nature of the subject matter changes the applicability of the analogy.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If Imam Zufar's view prevailed, the agent could divorce the wife at any future time, and the husband could revoke the delegation before the divorce occurs. Under the majority view, the agent's power is limited to the immediate session and is irrevocable.
The difference affects both temporal scope and the husband's control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تیسرا مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے کسی مرد سے کہا کہ اگر تو چاہے تو اسکویعنی میری بیوی کو طلاق دیدے تو اس صورت میں اس مرد کو صرف مجلس میں طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہو گا نہ کہ مجلس کے بعد ۔ اور شوہر کیلئے اپنے قول سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار بھی نہیں ہو گا ۔ امام زفر نے فرمایا کہ یہ قول یعنی طلقها ان شنت اور قول اول یعنی طلق امرأتی حکم میں دونوں برابر ہیں۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ مشیت کو صراحتا ذ کر نہ کرنا دونوں برابر ہیں۔ کیونکہ وہ آدمی جس کو طلق امراتی سے اختیار دیا ہے وہ اپنی مشیت ہی سے تصرف کرے گا۔ اسلئے کہ طلاق دینا اس اجنبی کا فعل ثانی بھی اول کی طرح تو کیل ہوگا۔ جیسا کہ وکیل با لبیچ سے کہا بعد ان شئت اور توکیل مجلس کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہوتی اور مؤکل کیلئے رجوع کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ پس اس طرح یہاں بھی طلاق دینے کا اختیار مجلس کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہوگا۔ اور مؤکل یعنی شوہر کیلئے اپنے قول سے رجوع کرنے کا اختیار حاصل ہوگا۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ قول ثانی طلقها ان شئت ایسی تملیک ہے جس میں تعلیق کے معنی پائے جاتے ہیں۔ پس معنی تملیک کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے یہ اختیار مجلس کے ساتھ مقید ہو گا۔ . تعلق ( یمین ) کے معنی کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے یہ تصرف لازم ہوگا۔ اور شوہر کو اپنے قول سے رجوع کا اختیار حاصل نہیں ہو گا۔ والطلاق يحتمل التعليق سے امام زفر کے قیاس کا جواب ہے۔ حاصل جواب یہ ہے کہ طلاق کو بیچ پر قیاس کرنا قیاس مع الفارق ہے۔ اسلئے کہ طلاق تعلیق کا احتمال رکھتی ہے اور بیچ تعلیق کا احتمال نہیں رکھتی ۔
The third issue is that if the husband said to a man: 'If you wish, divorce her, i.e., my wife,' then in this case that man only has the option to divorce in the session, not after the session, and the husband does not have the option to revoke his statement. Imam Zufar said that this statement 'divorce her if you wish' and the first statement 'divorce my wife' are equal in ruling. His proof is that not explicitly mentioning the condition is the same, because the man who is given option by 'divorce my wife' will act by his own wish anyway, so the act of divorce by this stranger, whether the second case or the first, will be wakalah. Just as in the case of a sale agent if one says 'sell if you wish,' the agency is not restricted to the session and the principal has the option to revoke. So here too the option to divorce will not be restricted to the session, and the principal (husband) will have the option to revoke his statement. Our proof is that the second statement 'divorce her if you wish' is a tamlik that contains the meaning of ta'liq (suspension). Considering the meaning of tamlik, the option is restricted to the session. Considering the meaning of ta'liq (like an oath), the action becomes binding, and the husband does not have the option to revoke his statement. And the statement 'divorce is open to suspension (ta'liq)' is a response to Imam Zufar's analogy. The summary of the response is that analogizing divorce to sale is qiyas ma'a al-fariq, because divorce can be suspended whereas sale cannot.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage discusses the third scenario where the husband tells a third party 'talliqha in shi'ta' (divorce her if you wish). The Hanafi position is that this is tamlik mixed with ta'liq, so it is treated like tamlik to the wife: the agent can only divorce in the session, and the husband cannot revoke. Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagreed and analogized it to the first case (talliq imra'ati), saying it is pure wakalah with an implied condition of wish. The Hanafi response distinguishes between wakalah and tamlik, and also notes that divorce can be suspended (ta'liq) while sale cannot, so analogy to sale fails.

Key Terms
طلقها ان شئت (talliqha in shi'ta): Phrase 'divorce her if you wish' used by husband to delegate divorce to a third party conditionally
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect (e.g., divorce) upon the occurrence of a condition
قياس مع الفارق (qiyas ma'a al-fariq): An analogy that is invalid because of a material difference between the two cases

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Delegation of Three Divorces: Wife Gives One
Ruling when husband delegates three divorces to wife and she gives only one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates three divorces and the wife gives one, and explain the legal reasoning that ownership of the greater includes the lesser.

Core Idea
Ownership of three includes ownership of one by logical necessity.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband gives his wife the authority to divorce herself three times, and she only effects one. The ruling clarifies that a valid divorce occurs, based on the principle that the power to do more necessarily includes the power to do less. Understanding this straightforward rule prevents confusion in delegation cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase
The husband uses the phrase 'talliqi nafsik thalathan' ('divorce yourself three times'), which grants the wife the authority (tamlik) to issue up to three divorces.
This is a valid form of tafwid (delegation) in Hanafi law.
2
Recognize the wife's action
The wife exercises this authority by saying she divorces herself only once, not three times.
She is not required to use all three; she may give fewer.
3
Apply the principle of darura (necessity)
Ownership of a right to effect three divorces necessarily implies ownership of the right to effect one, because the greater includes the lesser. This is a logical necessity (darura).
The Hanafi school uses this reasoning without need for additional evidence.
4
State the ruling
Therefore, one divorce occurs immediately upon her pronouncement. The divorce is valid and effective.
This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلقی تفسک ثلاثاً کہا عورت نے طلقت واحدۃ کہا تو ایک طلاق واقع ہوگی و لو قال لها طلقى نفسك ثلثا فطلقت واحدة فهى واحدة لانها ملكت ايقاع الثلث فتملك ايقاع الواحد ضرورة
(If the husband) said to (his wife): 'Divorce yourself three times,' then she divorced once, so it will be one, because she owned the right to effect three, so by necessity she owns the right to effect one.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: if the husband gives his wife the authority to divorce herself three times (tamlik of three divorces), and she only effects one, then one divorce occurs. The reasoning is that ownership of the greater includes ownership of the lesser. She had the right to give three, so she certainly has the right to give one. This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
طلقی نفسک ثلاثا (talliqi nafsik thalathan): Phrase 'divorce yourself three times' - tamlik of three divorces to the wife
ضرورة (darura): Necessity; here meaning logically necessary that ownership of greater implies ownership of lesser

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When a husband delegates authority for three divorces, the wife may validly effect any number up to three; giving one is effective because the greater includes the lesser.

Delegation of One Divorce: Wife Gives Three – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions on effect when wife gives three divorces instead of one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions on the effect when a wife gives three divorces after being delegated one, and explain the legal reasoning behind each side.

Core Idea
Exceeding delegated talaq authority: Abu Hanifa voids the extra, Sahibayn count one.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute. A husband says 'divorce yourself once,' but the wife gives three. Does any divorce occur? Abu Hanifa says no, because she acted beyond her mandate. The two companions say one divorce occurs, because she performed what was authorized and the extra is null. Understanding this disagreement sharpens your grasp of the nature of tamlik (delegation by ownership) versus direct authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The husband delegates one divorce to his wife by saying 'talliqi nafsik wahidatan'. She responds by pronouncing three divorces.
2
State the positions
Abu Hanifa: no divorce occurs. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): one divorce occurs.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
He argues she did what was not delegated, acting as a beginner (mubtadi'ah). Three is not one because three is composite, one is individual – they are opposite (mudadah).
Key concept: tamlik is restrictive.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
She did what she owned (one) and added more, so the extra is null. Analogy: husband divorcing a thousand times results in three.
They see the authority as a grant that allows at least one.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the marriage remains intact; under Sahibayn, one divorce counts, reducing the total remaining divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال لها طلقى نفسك واحدة فطلقت نفسها ثلثا لم يقع شيء عند ابي حنيفة وقالا يقع واحدة لإنها اتت بما ملكة و زيادة فصار كما اذا طلقها الزوج الفا و لابى حنيفة انها اتت بغير مافوض اليها فكانت مبتدأة وهذالان الزوج ملكها الواحدة والثلث غير الواحدة لان الثلث اسم لعدد مركب مجتمع والواحد فرد لاتركيب فيه فكانت بينهما مغايرة على سبيل المضادة بخلاف الزوج لانه يتصرف بحكم الملك
And if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself once,' and she divorced herself three times, nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said one occurs because she did what she owned and more, so it is like when the husband divorces her a thousand times. And for Abu Hanifa, (the reason is) that she did what was not delegated to her, so she was a beginner. And this is because the husband made her owner of one, and three is not one, because 'three' is a name for a composite number, while 'one' is an individual without composition, so between them there is opposition in a contrary sense, unlike the husband because he acts by right of ownership.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. If the husband grants his wife authority to divorce herself once (tamlik of one), and she instead gives three divorces, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that no divorce occurs because she exceeded her authority and acted as a beginner (mubtadi'ah) - she did not act on the delegated authority. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule that one divorce occurs because she did what she was authorized to do (one) and extra, so the extra is null. They analogize to a husband divorcing his wife a thousand times: only three count. Abu Hanifa distinguishes because the basis of the wife's authority is a grant (tamlik), which is restrictive, while the husband's authority is direct ownership.

Key Terms
مبتدأة (mubtadi'ah): Beginner; one who acts on her own rather than on the delegated authority, resulting in no effect
مضادة (mudadah): Opposition; contradiction; here between the nature of one and three

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsik wahidatan' and she gives three divorces, what is the result?
A) One divorce occurs
B) Three divorces occur
C) No divorce occurs
D) It depends on intent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the nature of delegation (tamlik) is restrictive; exceeding the limit voids the action according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn salvage one occurrence.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Husband Commands One Divorce, Wife Gives Three
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation exceeding authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa rules no divorce occurs when a wife exceeds the delegated number, and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Exceeding the delegated number voids the entire act because the authority is specific and the numbers are fundamentally opposed.

When a husband delegates a single divorce, his wife has authority only over that specific number. If she pronounces three, she has not merely added extra, but has performed a fundamentally different act according to Abu Hanifa. The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa rules nothing occurs, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) rule one divorce takes effect. This lesson examines the reasoning behind both positions and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and Hanafi positions
Clearly describe the case: husband says 'Divorce yourself once', wife says 'I divorce myself three times'. Note that Abu Hanifa says nothing occurs, Sahibayn say one occurs.
Remember that the dispute is purely intra-Hanafi.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's proof and analogy
The Sahibayn compare to a husband giving a thousand divorces: he owns three, so three occur and the rest are void. Similarly, here the wife owns one, so one occurs and the extra two are void.
This is a qiyas based on the principle that the principal act is effective.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's linguistic and legal objection
Abu Hanifa argues that one and three are not merely different quantities but opposite in nature: one is singular and uncompounded, three is composite. Therefore the wife did not perform the delegated act at all; she became a beginner (mubtadi'a).
The key is 'mugāyara ʿalā sabīl al-muḍādda' (opposition).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa: no divorce, marriage remains intact. Under Sahibayn: one revocable divorce occurs, wife must observe iddah.
The practical consequence is whether the marital tie is affected.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's, because delegation is strict and must be adhered to exactly.
This is from Al-Hidayah and later fatwa collections.
