And if he said to her, 'Divorce yourself once,' and she divorced herself three times, nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa, and they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said one occurs because she brought what she owned and an increase, so it is as if the husband divorced her a thousand times. And for Abu Hanifa, she brought what was not delegated to her, so she was a beginner. This is because the husband made her owner of one, and three is not one, because three is a name for a compound number, and one is a singular without composition, so there is opposition between them. Unlike the husband, because he acts by right of ownership. And likewise in the first case because she owned three, but here she does not own three and she did not bring what was delegated to her, so it is void. Translation: And if the husband said to his wife, 'Divorce yourself once,' and she effected three upon herself, then according to Abu Hanifa nothing occurs, and the two companions said one occurs because she brought what she owns plus an increase, so it became as if the husband divorced her a thousand times. Abu Hanifa's proof is that she brought what was not delegated to her, so she is a beginner. This is because the husband made her owner of one, and three is other than one, because three is the name of a compound collective number, and one is an individual with no composition, so there is opposition between one and three. Unlike the husband, because he acts by virtue of ownership. And similarly in the first case because she owned three, but here she does not own three, and she did not bring what was delegated to her, so it is void. Explanation: The scenario is that if the husband told his wife 'Divorce yourself once' and she effected three divorces upon herself, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa no divorce occurs—this is also the view of Imam Zufar and Imam Malik. According to the two companions, one occurs—and this is also the view of Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad. The proof of the two companions is that the wife effected what the husband made her owner of and added extra, so it is like the husband saying 'Divorce yourself' and she divorces herself and her co-wife—what was delegated occurs and the extra is void. Thus in this case one divorce occurs and the remaining two are void. This is like if the husband himself gave his wife a thousand divorces, then three divorces he legally owns occur and the rest are void. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that she did not effect what she owned, because the husband made her owner of one, and three is other than one, because three is a compound number and one is a singular without composition, so there is opposition between one and three. Therefore, when she effected other than the delegated divorce, she became a beginner, and when a woman initiates a divorce upon herself, it does not occur unless the husband gives permission. In contrast, if the husband gave his wife a thousand divorces, three occur because he speaks by virtue of ownership—he can effect as many as he wishes but only three are effective. Similarly, in the first case the woman also spoke by virtue of ownership because she owned three and effected one upon herself. In this case she does not own three and she did not effect what was delegated (one), so due to lack of correspondence between the husband's statement and the wife's answer, the wife's statement is void.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a husband delegating a single divorce to his wife, who then pronounces three divorces. The passage presents the ruling, the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), and the reasoning for each. 

First, the ruling: If a husband says to his wife, 'Divorce yourself once,' and she says 'I have divorced myself three times,' then according to Imam Abu Hanifa no divorce occurs at all. According to the two companions, one divorce occurs (the remaining two are void). 

The two companions compare this to a husband giving a thousand divorces: he owns three, so three occur and the rest are void. Similarly here, the wife owns one divorce (the delegated authority), so one occurs and the extra two are void. 

Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the wife's action is fundamentally different from the husband's. The husband owns the right of divorce completely; his pronouncement creates divorces up to three. The wife, however, only has delegated authority over exactly one divorce. 'Three' is not a mere quantity distinct from 'one'—it is a composite number, while 'one' is single and uncompounded. So when she says 'three,' she has not performed the delegated act at all; she has initiated a new act of divorce. A woman initiating divorce without delegation is ineffective unless the husband later ratifies it. Therefore, nothing occurs. 

The passage also notes that this differs from a previous case (likely the case where the husband delegated three divorces and she gave one) where the wife owned three and thus her action was valid. Here she does not own three. 

The teacher should emphasize the distinction between ownership of a specific right versus a general right. The Hanafi principle is that delegation (tafwid) is strict: the agent must act within the scope of authority. Exceeding the scope renders the entire act void, not partially valid. This is because the authority is specific, not general. Also note that the two companions' analogy to the husband's multiple divorces is weak because the husband's ownership is original, not delegated.

Key Terms
طلقى نفسك (ṭalliqī nafsak): Husband delegating the power of divorce to his wife for a specific number.
مبتدأة (mubtadiʾa): A woman who initiates divorce on herself without valid delegation from husband.
مغايرة على سبيل المضادة (mugāyara ʿalā sabīl al-muḍādda): Complete opposition between one and three such that they cannot be reconciled as same category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does no divorce occur when a wife, delegated one divorce, pronounces three?
A) Because she acted without intention.
B) Because three is not one due to opposition in composition.
C) Because the husband did not witness.
D) Because the wife is not qualified to give divorce.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife's delegated authority for divorce is strictly limited to the specified number; exceeding it voids the entire action, not just the excess.

Module 4 · Lesson 266

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
Husband Commands Revocable, Wife Gives Irrevocable or Vice Versa
Ruling on discrepancy in description of divorce in delegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife's description of divorce differs from her husband's command and explain why the husband's description controls.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, the husband's specification of description binds; the wife's contrary description is void.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario in delegated divorce (tafwīḍ): a husband authorizes his wife to pronounce a divorce of a specific type, but the wife pronounces a different type. The ruling is straightforward but reveals a key principle about agency and the distinction between the principal (aṣl) and description (waṣf). Understanding this helps clarify the limits of a wife's authority when delegated to divorce herself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, internalize the two cases: (1) husband commands a revocable divorce, wife gives irrevocable; (2) husband commands irrevocable, wife gives revocable.
Each scenario has the same number (one), only the description differs.
2
Recognize the principal vs. description
In tafwid, the wife is an agent. The act of divorce itself is the principal (aṣl); the specified type (revocable/irrevocable) is the description (waṣf). The agent must execute the principal as instructed; deviations in description are extra.
The description is secondary and does not affect the validity of the principal act.
3
Apply the principle
The wife's contrary description is void (lāghū) because it contradicts the husband's command. The divorce occurs with the description he commanded, regardless of what she said.
This applies even if her description is more restrictive (irrevocable) or less restrictive (revocable).
4
Conclude the resulting divorce type
In case 1, a revocable divorce occurs; in case 2, an irrevocable divorce occurs. The wife's wording does not alter the legal effect of the husband's delegation.
The same ruling would apply if the wife added a condition or other extra element not commanded.

ترجمہ اور اگر ) مرد نے ) عورت کو ایسی طلاق کا حکم مہ( جس میں ) رجعت کر سکتا ہے۔ پس عورت نے (اپنے آپ کو ) بائنہ طلاق دے دی یا شوہر نے اس کو طلاق بائن کا حکم دیا اور اس نے طلاق رجعی دی تو وہی واقع ہوگی جس کا شوہر نے حکم دیا ہے۔ پس مسئلہ اول کی صورت یہ ہے کہ شوہر عورت سے کہے تو اپنے نفس کو ایک ایسی طلاق دے کہ میں رجعت کا مالک رہوں ۔ پس وہ عورت کہتی ہے کہ میں نے پنے نفس کو ایک بائن دی ، تو ایک رجعیہ واقع ہوگی کیونکہ عورت اصل کو زیادتی وصف کے ساتھ لائی۔ جیسا کہ میں نے ذکر کیا۔ پس وصف الغو ہو گیا اور اصل باقی رہے گی ۔ اور دوسرے مسئلہ کے معنی یہ ہے کہ (شوہر) عورت سے کہے تو اپنے نفس کو ایک طلاق بائن دیدے۔ پس عورت کہتی کہ میں نے اپنے نفس کو ایک رجعیہ دی تو بائنہ واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عورت کا قول واحدۃ رجعیتہ اس کی طرف سے لغو حرکت ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر نے جو طلاق عورت کے سپرد کی ہے اس کا وصف متعین کر دیا۔ تو اب اس کے بعد عورت کی حاجت اصل طلاق واقع کرنے میں ہے ( اس کا ) وصف متعین کرنے میں نہیں۔ تو ایسا ہوا گویا کہ عورت نے اصل طلاق پر اکتفاء کیا۔ پس ( طلاق ) اسی صفت کے ساتھ واقع ہوگی جس کو شوہر نے متعین کیا ہے۔ ہائن ہو یا ر جعی۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق رجعی کا اختیار دیا اسکی بیوی نے اپنے اوپر طلاق بائن واقع کی یا شوہر نے اس کو طلاق بائن کا حکم دیا۔ اس نے طلاق رجعی واقع کی عرض یہ کہ عورت نے وصف طلاق میں اپنے شوہر کی مخالفت کی ہے۔ پس ان دونوں صورتوں میں وہی طلاق واقع ہوگی ۔ جس کا شوہر نے اس کو حکم دیا ہے۔ پہلے مسئلہ کی صورت یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا طلقی نفسك واحدة أملک الرجعة ... بیوی نے جواب میں کہا طلقت نفسى واحدة بائنہ تو اس صورت میں ایک رجعی واقع ہو
And if he commands her with a divorce in which he retains the right of return, and she gives an irrevocable divorce, or he commands her with an irrevocable divorce and she gives a revocable one, what the husband commanded occurs. The meaning of the first: the husband says to her, 'Divorce yourself with a revocable divorce' and she says 'I have divorced myself once irrevocably,' so a revocable divorce occurs because she brought the principal and an extra description, as we mentioned, so the description is void and the principal remains. The meaning of the second: he says, 'Divorce yourself once irrevocably' and she says 'I have divorced myself once revocably,' so an irrevocable divorce occurs because her statement 'once revocably' is void from her, because the husband specified the description of what was delegated to her, so her need after that is to effect the principal without specifying the description, so it becomes as if she confined herself to the principal, so it occurs with the description that the husband specified, whether irrevocable or revocable. Translation: And if the husband commands the wife with a divorce where he retains the right to take her back, and she gives an irrevocable divorce, or he commands her with an irrevocable divorce and she gives a revocable one, then that which the husband commanded occurs. The first case: the husband says to her, 'Divorce yourself with a revocable divorce,' and she says 'I have divorced myself once irrevocably,' then a revocable divorce occurs because she brought the principal with an extra description, so the description is void and the principal remains. The second case: he says, 'Divorce yourself once irrevocably,' and she says 'I have divorced myself once revocably,' then an irrevocable divorce occurs because her statement 'once revocably' is void from her, because the husband specified the description of what was delegated to her, so she only needs to effect the principal without specifying the description, so it is as if she limited herself to the principal, so it occurs with the description the husband specified, whether revocable or irrevocable. Explanation: The scenario is that the husband gave his wife the option of a revocable divorce, and she effected an irrevocable divorce upon herself, or the husband commanded her to give an irrevocable divorce and she gave a revocable one. The summary is that the wife opposed the description of the divorce. So in both cases, the divorce that the husband commanded occurs. The first case: the husband said 'Divorce yourself once revocably,' and the wife answered 'I have divorced myself once irrevocably,' then one revocable divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about the effect of a wife's deviation from the description of divorce delegated by her husband. The ruling is clear: the husband's specification of whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable controls. The wife's alteration of the description is void, and the divorce takes effect with the description commanded by the husband.

There are two cases: (1) Husband commands a revocable divorce, wife pronounces an irrevocable one. Since the wife is merely an agent, her adding the description 'bāʾin' (irrevocable) is an extra description contrary to the husband's command. Following the principle established earlier, the extra description (waṣf) is void, and the principal (aṣl) divorce occurs, which is revocable. (2) Husband commands an irrevocable divorce, wife pronounces a revocable one. Here, the wife's description is 'rajʿī' (revocable), which is less than what the husband commanded. Since the husband specified the description, the wife's need is only to effect the divorce itself; the description is irrelevant because it contradicts the husband's specification. Therefore, her statement 'rajʿiyyah' is void, and the divorce occurs as irrevocable.

The reason for the difference from the previous case (where she exceeded the number) is that here the number is the same (one), only the description differs. The description is subordinate to the principal, and in delegation, the principal is what matters. The husband's authority over the description is complete; the wife cannot alter it. This ruling applies regardless of whether the wife's description is more restrictive (bāʾin) or less restrictive (rajʿī) than commanded.

This is a straightforward application of agency law in Hanafi fiqh: the agent must act exactly within the scope of authority, and any variation in non-material aspects (like description) is disregarded in favor of the principal's command.

Key Terms
أصل (aṣl): The principal divorce (the act of divorcing), as opposed to its description.
وصف (waṣf): Description of the divorce (revocable or irrevocable) that may be added by the agent but is void if contrary to command.
بائن (bāʾin): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately and allows no revocation without new marriage.
رجعی (rajʿī): Revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband commands his wife to give a revocable divorce, and she pronounces an irrevocable divorce, which type of divorce occurs?
A) Revocable (rajʿī)
B) Irrevocable (bāʾin)
C) Both are void
D) It depends on the wife's intention

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the husband's description of the divorce is binding; any contrary description by the wife is void, and the divorce takes effect as commanded.

Talaq — Divorce

Ruling on Mismatch of Description in Delegated Divorce
Ruling statement on description mismatch in tafwid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife gives a different type of divorce than authorized, and apply it to both scenarios of raj'i and bain.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, the wife is an agent; her description is void, the husband's type prevails.

When a husband delegates divorce to his wife with a specific type, she must execute that exact type. If she adds or changes the description, it is ignored. This lesson explains the ruling with two clear examples from the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's authorization
Determine what type of divorce the husband commanded (e.g., raj'i or bain).
The husband specifies the description (wasf) in his delegation.
2
Identify the wife's pronouncement
See what the wife actually said (e.g., bain when told raj'i, or raj'i when told bain).
Her statement includes both the original (asl) and a different description.
3
Apply the agency principle
The wife acts as an agent (wakil). She does not own the authority to change the description. Her added description is void (laghw).
She must effect the original as commanded.
4
Conclude the divorce type
The divorce occurs with the husband's specified type, regardless of what the wife said.
Result: if husband said raj'i, raj'i occurs; if bain, bain occurs.

الا یہ کہ مرد اجازت دیدے. اس کے برخلاف جب شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو ایک ہزار طلاقیں دیدیں تو تین واقع ہو جائیں گی۔ کیونکہ شوہر مالک ہونے کی حیثیت سے طلاق کا تکلم کرتا ہے۔ پس جتنی چاہے واقع کرے مگر نافذ بقدر حل ہوں گی ۔ اس طرح پہلے مسئلہ میں عورت بھی مالک ہونے کی حیثیت سے تکلم کرتی ہے کیونکہ تین کی مالک تھی۔ اور اپنے اوپر ایک واقع کی ہے۔ اور اس مسئلہ میں وہ تین کی مالک نہیں ہے۔ اور جو اس کے سپرد کی گئی تھی یعنی ایک وہ اس نے واقع نہیں کی۔ لہذا مرد کے قول اور عورت کے جواب میں موافقت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے عورت کا قول لغو ہو جائے گا۔ شوہر نے طلاق رجعی دینے کا کہا اس نے بائنہ دی یا بائنہ دینے کا کہا اس نے رجعی طلاق دی مرد نے جس طلاق کا حکم کیا وہی واقع ہوگی

ترجمہ اور اگر مرد نے عورت کو ایسی طلاق کا حکم کیا جس میں رجعت کر سکتا ہے۔ پس عورت نے اپنے آپ کو بائنہ طلاق دے دی یا شوہر نے اس کو طلاق بائن کا حکم دیا اور اس نے طلاق رجعی دی تو وہی واقع ہوگی جس کا شوہر نے حکم دیا ہے۔ پس مسئلہ اول کی صورت یہ ہے کہ شوہر عورت سے کہے تو اپنے نفس کو ایک ایسی طلاق دے کہ میں رجعت کا مالک رہوں ۔ پس وہ عورت کہتی ہے کہ میں نے اپنے نفس کو ایک بائن دی، تو ایک رجعیہ واقع ہوگی کیونکہ عورت اصل کو زیادتی وصف کے ساتھ لائی۔ جیسا کہ میں نے ذکر کیا۔ پس وصف الغو ہو گیا اور اصل باقی رہے گی ۔ اور دوسرے مسئلہ کے معنی یہ ہے کہ شوہر عورت سے کہے تو اپنے نفس کو ایک طلاق بائن دیدے۔ پس عورت کہتی کہ میں نے اپنے نفس کو ایک رجعیہ دی تو بائنہ واقع ہوگی۔ کیونکہ عورت کا قول واحدۃ رجعیتہ اس کی طرف سے لغو حرکت ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر نے جو طلاق عورت کے سپرد کی ہے اس کا وصف متعین کر دیا۔ تو اب اس کے بعد عورت کی حاجت اصل طلاق واقع کرنے میں ہے (اس کا) وصف متعین کرنے میں نہیں۔ تو ایسا ہوا گویا کہ عورت نے اصل طلاق پر اکتفاء کیا۔ پس (طلاق) اسی صفت کے ساتھ واقع ہوگی جس کو شوہر نے متعین کیا ہے۔ بائن ہو یا رجععی۔

Book of Divorce. Except if the husband gives permission. Conversely, if a husband gives his wife a thousand divorces, three occur. Because the husband speaks as owner of divorce. So as many as he wishes may occur, but only effective to the extent possible. Similarly, in the first issue, the wife also speaks as owner because she owned three. And she made one occur upon herself. In this issue, she does not own three. And what was entrusted to her—i.e., one—she did not make it occur. Therefore, due to lack of correspondence between the husband's statement and the wife's response, the wife's statement becomes null. If the husband said to give a raj'i divorce and she gave a bain divorce, or he said to give a bain and she gave raj'i, the divorce occurs as the husband commanded. The meaning of the first: the husband says to her 'Divorce yourself with one divorce in which I have the right of return.' She says 'I divorced myself with one bain divorce.' So a raj'i divorce occurs because she came with the original plus an extra description, as we mentioned. So the description becomes null and the original remains. The meaning of the second: he says 'Divorce yourself with one bain divorce.' She says 'I divorced myself with one raj'i divorce.' So a bain divorce occurs because her saying 'one raj'i' from her is null. Because the husband specified the description of what was delegated to her. So after that, her need is only to effect the original without specifying the description. Thus it is as if she confined herself to the original, so it occurs with the description the husband specified, whether bain or raj'i. Translation: And if the husband commanded her a divorce in which he can return, and she gave herself a bain divorce, or he commanded her a bain and she gave raj'i, then that occurs which the husband commanded. The first case: the husband says to her 'Divorce yourself with one divorce in which I have the right of return.' She says 'I divorced myself with one bain.' So a raj'i occurs because she brought the original with an extra description. The description is nullified and the original remains. The second case: the husband says 'Divorce yourself with one bain.' She says 'I divorced myself with one raj'i.' So a bain occurs because her 'one raj'i' is null from her. Because the husband specified the description of the delegated divorce. So after that, her need is to effect the original, not to specify the description. So it is as if she confined herself to the original, so it occurs with the description the husband specified, whether bain or raj'i. Explanation: The scenario is that the husband gave his wife authority to give a raj'i divorce, and she made a bain divorce upon herself, or the husband commanded her a bain and she gave raj'i. The point is that the wife opposed the husband in the description. In both cases, the divorce occurs as the husband commanded. For the first issue: the husband said to his wife 'Divorce yourself with one divorce in which I have the right of return.' The wife responded 'I divorced myself with one bain.' In this case, one raj'i occurs.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage from the book of divorce (kitab al-talaq) explains a ruling concerning delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The author begins by contrasting a scenario where the husband directly pronounces many divorces—only three count—with the scenario of delegation. The focus here is when a husband authorizes his wife to divorce herself with a specific type (e.g., raj'i or bain), but the wife pronounces the opposite type. The ruling is that the husband's command prevails; the wife's contrary description is considered invalid (laghw). The reasoning is that the wife is acting as an agent (wakil) and does not own the power to change the description. She must execute the original (asl) divorce as commanded, and any additional description she adds is superfluous. Therefore, if the husband authorizes a raj'i divorce and she gives bain, a raj'i divorce occurs, and vice versa. The Arabic matn and Urdu sharh both clarify this principle with examples. The passage emphasizes that the wife's authority is limited to executing the type specified by the husband; she cannot alter it. This is a straightforward application of agency law in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
طلاق رجعی (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take the wife back during the waiting period without a new contract.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that immediately ends the marriage; the husband cannot take back his wife except with a new marriage contract.
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce from the husband to the wife, giving her authority to pronounce divorce on herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A raj'i divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the husband's specified description is decisive; any contrary description by the wife is void, and the divorce occurs as he commanded.

Ruling on Conditional Response in Delegated Divorce
Explains rulings on wife's conditional response in delegated divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife responds with a conditional willing in delegated divorce and explain why the delegated authority lapses.

Core Idea
A wife's conditional willing in delegated divorce is futile; the authority is lost and no divorce occurs.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in delegated divorce (tafwid): when the husband delegates the option to divorce by saying 'You are divorced if you wish,' and the wife responds with a condition such as 'I wish if you wish' or 'I wish if my father wishes.' The Hanafi ruling is that the wife's conditional response is not an acceptance of the delegation; it is futile speech, and she loses the delegated authority. The lesson also explores the linguistic distinction between 'shi'tu' (I caused) and 'aradtu' (I intended) in effecting divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation
The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' (anti tāliq in shi'ti). This makes divorce conditional on the wife's unconditional willing. Note that the husband's condition is a pure will, not tied to any other condition.
2
Analyze the wife's response
The wife responds with a conditional statement like 'I wish if you wish' (shi'tu in shi'ta). She has made her willing conditional on the husband's will, which is not what was delegated. This is called mashi'a mu'allaqa (conditional willing) as opposed to mashi'a mursala (unconditional).
3
Determine legal effect
Since the wife did not produce unconditional willing, the condition for divorce is not met. Moreover, she engaged in futile speech (laghw) indicating disinterest (i'rad), which causes the delegated authority to lapse. No divorce occurs, and the wife cannot later use the authority.
If the wife conditions on a past event (e.g., 'I wish if such and such happened'), it is treated as immediate because the condition is already fulfilled.
4
Distinguish between shi'tu and aradtu
If the husband independently says 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqak), divorce occurs because shi'tu implies causing existence. But 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqak) does not cause divorce because intention alone does not effect divorce. This distinction is linguistic: shi'tu is derived from shay' (existent), while irada means seeking.

جدید واقع کرنا ہو گا۔ اس وجہ سے کہ مشیت وجود کی خبر دیتی ہے۔ اس کے برخلاف اس کا قول کہ میں نے تیری طلاق کا اردہ کیا۔ کیونکہ ارادہ وجود کی خبر نہیں دیتا۔ اور یونہی (طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی) جب عورت نے کہا چاہا میں نے اگر میرے باپ نے چاہا۔ یا چاہا میں نے اگر ایسا ہو (مشروط کیا) ایسے امر پر جو ابھی تک موجود نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اس وجہ سے جو ہم نے ذکر کیا ہے کہ وہ جو مشیت لائی ہے وہ مشیت معلقہ ہے۔ پس طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی اور امر بالید باطل ہو گیا۔ اور اگر عورت نے کہا میں نے چاہا اگر ایسا ہوا ہو۔ (مشروط کیا) ایسے امر کے ساتھ جو کہ گذر چکا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ ایسی چیز پر معلق کرنا جو ہو چکی تجیز ہے (تعلیق نہیں ہے)۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت طالق ان شئت عورت نے کہا شنت ان شئت ۔ اور مرد طلاق کی نیت بھی کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں امر بالید باطل ہو گیا۔ اور عورت کیلئے اختیار باقی نہیں رہا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کی طلاق کو مشیت غیر معلقہ پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور عورت لائی مشیت معلقہ یعنی اپنے چاہنے کو شوہر کے چاہنے پر معلق کیا ہے۔ پس شرط کے نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی اور چونکہ عورت لایعنی کام میں مشغول ہوگئی جو دلیل اعراض ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اس کے ہاتھ سے امر بھی نکل گیا۔ اور اختیار باقی نہیں رہا (لایعنی کام یہ ہے کہ عورت نے اپنی مشیت کو شوہر کی مشیت پر معلق کیا ہے)۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر عورت کے قول شئتُ ان شئت کے جواب میں مرد نے شئت کہہ دیا تو اس لفظ شئت سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ اگر چہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہو۔ دلیل یہ ہے عورت کے کلام شئت ان شئت میں طلاق کا ذکر نہیں ہے تاکہ شوہر اسکی طلاق کو چاہنے والا ہوتا۔ اور اگر آپ کہیں کہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نیت کا عمل مذکور میں ہوتا ہے نہ کہ غیر مذکور میں ۔ اور یہاں نہ عورت کے کلام میں طلاق مذکور ہے اور نہ مرد کے کلام میں۔ اسلئے نیت کے باوجود طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ چنانچہ اگر شوہر نے شئت طلاقک کہا اور طلاق کی نیت بھی کی ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ لیکن یہ طلاق کا واقع ہونا اسلئے نہیں کہ جواب ہے بلکہ اسلئے ہے کہ ابتداء طلاق واقع کی گئی۔
مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ شئت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ لیکن اردت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ شئت ماخوذ ہے شیء سے اور شیء کے معنی ہیں موجود کے پس شئت، اوجدت کے معنی میں ہوگا۔ اور ایجاد طلاق بغیر ایقاع کے نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اس لفظ شئت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور ارادہ کے معنی ہیں طلب کے۔ جیسا کہ حدیث میں فرمایا گیا احدھما زائد الموت ۔ پس اب اردت طلاقک کے معنی ہوں گے طلبت طلاقک اور یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ طلب طلاق سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی ۔ اس وجہ سے اس لفظ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
اور اگر عورت نے انت طالق ان شئت کے جواب میں شئت ان شاء ابی کہہ دیا یا شئت ان كان كذا کہا۔ یعنی ایسے امر پر معلق کر دیا جو ابھی تک نہیں ہوا۔ تو اس صورت میں بھی طلاق واقع نہ ہوگی۔ دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی کہ عورت مشیت معلقہ لائی۔ حالانکہ طلاق مشیت مرسلہ (غیر معلقہ) پر متعلق ہے۔ اور لایعنی کام میں مشغول ہونے کی وجہ سے عورت کا اختیار بھی باطل ہو گیا۔ اور اگر عورت نے مرد کے کلام کے جواب میں کہا شئت ان كان كذا یعنی ایسے امر پر معلق کیا جو زمانہ ماضی میں ہو چکا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ طلاق کو ایسی شرط پر معلق کرنا جو شرط پہلے سے موجود ہے۔ طلاق منجز کہلائے گی جیسے کسی نے کہا انت طالق ان كان السماء فوقنا ۔اس صورت میں طلاق منجز یعنی فی الحال واقع ہوگی۔
It must occur anew. Because 'willing' (mashi'a) reports existence. In contrast, his saying 'I intended your divorce' because intention does not report existence. Similarly, divorce does not occur when the wife says 'I willed if my father willed' or 'I willed if such and such happens'—conditioning on something not yet existent. For the reason we mentioned: the conditional willing (mashi'a mu'allaqa) she brought is not the unconditional willing required. So divorce does not occur and the command (amr bil-yad) becomes void. If the wife says 'I willed if such and such had happened'—conditioning on something past—then divorce occurs. Because conditional upon something already existent is not true conditioning but immediate (tanjiz). Explanation: If the husband says to his wife 'You are divorced if you wish,' and she says 'I wish if you wish,' and the husband also intends divorce, then in this case the command becomes void and the wife's authority is lost. The proof: the husband made the divorce conditional upon unconditional willing (mashi'a mursala), and she brought conditional willing, making her will conditional upon the husband's will. So due to the condition not being found, divorce does not occur. Since she engaged in futile speech (laghw), which is an indication of disinterest (i'rad), she loses the authority. The author of al-Hidayah says: if the husband says 'I wish' in response to the wife's 'I wish if you wish,' divorce does not occur from that word, even if he intended divorce. The reason is that his speech does not mention divorce. If you say he intended, we respond: intention applies only to what is mentioned. Here divorce is mentioned in neither speech. Therefore, despite intention, divorce does not occur. However, if the husband says 'I wish your divorce' with intention, divorce occurs; but this is not as a response but as an independent pronouncement. The author says: 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqak) causes divorce, but 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqak) does not. The difference: shi'tu is derived from shay' (thing) meaning existence, so shi'tu means 'I brought into existence.' And creating divorce is inseparable from effecting it. Therefore, it causes divorce. 'Irada' means seeking (talab), as in the hadith 'one of them sought death.' So 'aradtu talaqak' means 'I sought your divorce,' and seeking divorce does not cause divorce. Hence, divorce does not occur. And if the wife responds to 'You are divorced if you wish' with 'I wish if my father wishes' or 'I wish if such and such happens'—conditioning on something not yet existent—then divorce also does not occur. The proof already mentioned: she brought conditional willing, while divorce was conditioned upon unconditional willing. She engaged in futile speech, so her authority becomes void. If she says 'I wish if such and such had happened'—conditioning on something in the past—divorce occurs. Because conditioning on something already existent is not conditional but immediate (munjiz). For example, if one says 'You are divorced if the sky is above us,' it is an immediate divorce.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on delegated divorce (tafwid), focusing on the wife's response when the husband uses the conditional phrase 'if you wish' (in shi't). The key principle is that the husband's delegation must be matched by a similar form; if the wife responds with a conditional statement that introduces a new condition (like 'if you wish' or 'if my father wishes'), she has not accepted the authority properly. Instead, she engaged in futile speech (laghw), which shows disinterest (i'rad), causing her to lose the delegated authority. The passage also distinguishes between the words 'shi'tu' (I wished/caused) and 'aradtu' (I intended). The linguistic root of 'shi'tu' implies creation (ijad), so 'shi'tu talaqak' actually effects divorce, while 'aradtu' only indicates desire and does not effect divorce. The author also clarifies that conditioning on a past event is not true conditioning but an immediate divorce (munjiz) because the condition is already fulfilled. The overall rule is that the wife must execute the divorce exactly as delegated; any deviation, especially introducing conditions, invalidates the authority and no divorce occurs unless the husband's own conditional is properly fulfilled.

Key Terms
مشیت (mashi'a): Willing or wishing; a declaration of volition that can be unconditional (mursala) or conditional (mu'allaqa).
مشیت معلقہ (mashi'a mu'allaqa): Conditional willing, e.g., 'I wish if you wish.' This does not fulfill the husband's unconditional delegation.
امر بالید (amr bil-yad): The authority given to the wife to divorce herself upon her own hand.
شئت (shi'tu): I wished/caused; derived from shay' (existent), implies bringing into existence.
اردت (aradtu): I intended; only indicates desire, does not effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A husband tells his wife 'You are divorced if you wish.' She responds 'I wish if my father wishes.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Divorce occurs immediately
B) Divorce occurs only if the father wishes
C) Divorce does not occur and the wife loses the delegated authority
D) The husband must repeat the delegation

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the wife must execute exactly as delegated; any conditional response nullifies the authority and no divorce occurs.

Talaq — Divorce

Effect of Wife's Conditional Response on Suspended Divorce
Ruling on effect of wife's conditional response on suspended divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a wife's conditional response ('I wish if you wish') invalidates a suspended divorce and voids her delegated authority.

Core Idea
A conditional response changes the condition — divorce does not occur and the option is lost.

The husband delegates the option to the wife by saying 'You are divorced if you wish.' This is a suspended divorce conditioned on the wife's unconditional will. But when the wife replies 'I wish if you wish,' she introduces a new condition, making her will dependent on the husband's. This alters the original condition, so the divorce does not take effect. Moreover, the wife's engagement in a futile act (laghw) shows aversion, causing her to forfeit the command (amr bil-yad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's utterance
The husband says 'ant taliq in shi'ta' — a suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq) conditioned on the wife's unconditional will.
The condition is the wife's will, which is mashiyyah mursalah (unconditional).
2
Analyze the wife's response
She says 'shi'tu in shi'ta' — she makes her will conditional on the husband's will (mashiyyah mu'allaqah).
This introduces a new condition not present in the husband's statement.
3
Determine the legal effect
Since the wife's will is now conditional, the original condition (her unconditional will) is not fulfilled. Therefore, the suspended divorce does not occur.
The condition must be fulfilled exactly as specified.
4
Assess the consequence on delegated authority
The wife's failure to respond properly is a futile act (laghw) which indicates aversion (i'rad). As a result, the amr bil-yad is voided and she loses the option.
The command leaves her hand because she engaged in laghw.

اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت طالق ان شئت عورت نے کہا شنت ان شئت ۔ اور مرد طلاق کی نیت بھی کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں امر بالید باطل ہو گیا۔ اور عورت کیلئے اختیار باقی نہیں رہا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کی طلاق کو مشیت غیر معلقہ پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور عورت لائی مشیمت معلقہ یعنی اپنے چاہنے کو شوہر کے چاہنے پر معلق کیا ہے۔ پس شرط کے نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی اور چونکہ عورت لایعنی کام میں مشغول ہوگئی جو دلیل اعراض ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اس کے ہاتھ سے امر بھی نکل گیا۔ اور اختیار باقی نہیں رہا ( لا یعنی کام یہ ہے کہ عورت نے اپنی مشئیت کو شوہر کی مشیت پر معلق کیا ہے )۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر عورت کے قول شئتُ ان شئت کے جواب میں مرد نے شئت کہہ دیا تو اس لفظ شئت سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ اگر چہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہو۔ دلیل یہ ہے عورت کے کلام شئت ان شئت میں طلاق کا ذکر نہیں ہے تاکہ شوہر اسکی طلاق کو چاہنے والا ہوتا۔ اور اگر آپ کہیں کہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نیت کا عمل مذکور میں ہوتا ہے نہ کہ غیر مذکور میں ۔ اور یہاں نہ عورت کے کلام میں طلاق مذکور ہے اور نہ مرد کے کلام میں۔ اسلئے نیت کے باوجود طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ چنانچہ اگر شوہر نے شدت طلاقک کہا اور طلاق کی نیت بھی کی ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ لیکن یہ طلاق کا واقع ہونا اسلئے نہیں کہ جواب ہے بلکہ اسلئے ہے کہ ابتداء طلاق واقع کی گئی۔
If the husband said to his wife: 'You are divorced if you wish,' and the woman said: 'I wish if you wish,' and the man also intends divorce, then in this case the command (amr bil-yad) becomes void, and no option remains for the woman. The proof is that the husband suspended his wife's divorce on an unconditional will (mashiyyah ghayr mu'allaqah), and the woman brought a conditional will (mashiyyah mu'allaqah), meaning she made her own will conditional on the husband's will. Thus, due to the condition not being fulfilled, divorce does not occur, and because the woman engaged in a futile act (laghw) which is evidence of aversion (i'rad), the command also leaves her hand, and no option remains. The author of Hidayah states: If in response to the woman's statement 'I wish if you wish,' the man said 'I wish,' then divorce does not occur from this word 'I wish,' even if the husband intended divorce. The proof is that in the woman's statement 'I wish if you wish,' there is no mention of divorce such that the husband would be intending her divorce. And if you say that the husband intended divorce, we will respond that intention operates on what is mentioned (madhkur), not on what is not mentioned. Here, divorce is neither mentioned in the woman's speech nor in the man's. Therefore, despite intention, divorce does not occur. However, if the husband said 'I wish your divorce' and also intended divorce, then divorce does occur. But this occurrence of divorce is not because it is a response, but because it is an initiation of divorce.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on conditional divorce when the wife makes her wish conditional on the husband's wish. The teacher explains: The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' (ant taliq in shi'ta). This is a suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq) where the condition is the wife's will, and her will is unconditioned (mashiyyah mursalah). If the wife responds, 'I wish if you wish' (shi'tu in shi'ta), she introduces an additional condition (her wish is conditional on the husband's wish). Since the original condition (wife's wish) is not realized as an independent event, the divorce does not occur. Moreover, because the wife engaged in a futile act (laghw) by not complying properly, the option (amr bil-yad) that the husband had given her is voided. The text also addresses a scenario where the husband says 'I wish' in response. Even if he intended divorce, that word alone does not effect divorce because 'I wish' is not an expression of talaq. Talaq only occurs if the husband explicitly says 'I wish your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), which is a direct and immediate pronouncement. This distinction highlights the Hanafi principle that the word used must be explicit for divorce to take effect in such contexts.

Key Terms
مشیت معلقہ (mashiyyah mu'allaqah): A conditional will; one's wish made dependent on another condition.
امر بالید (amr bil-yad): The matter placed in the woman's hand; the option or authority given to her to pronounce divorce.

C) Talaq does not occur and the wife loses the delegated option.

Key Takeaway
In a suspended divorce conditioned on the wife's unconditional will, her conditional response ('I wish if you wish') invalidates the divorce and voids her delegated authority.

Effect of Husband Saying 'I Wish' Without Explicit Divorce Expression
Ruling on whether husband's 'I wish' constitutes divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the word 'I wish' alone, even with intention of divorce, does not effect divorce unless the divorce is explicitly mentioned.

Core Idea
Intention alone cannot create divorce where the word used is not an expression of talaq.

In the continuation of the previous scenario, if the husband responds to the wife's 'I wish if you wish' by saying 'I wish' (shi'tu), does this constitute a divorce? The author of Al-Hidayah clarifies that it does not. The word 'I wish' is not an expression of divorce; it is ambiguous. Even if the husband intended divorce, his intention cannot operate on an absent (ghayr madhkur) expression. Divorce only occurs if the husband explicitly says 'I wish your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), which is a direct pronouncement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's utterance
The husband says 'I wish' (shi'tu) in response to the wife's conditional statement.
The word 'shi'tu' alone is not a recognized lafz al-talaq.
2
Recognize the scope of intention
Apply the principle: intention (niyyah) operates only on what is mentioned (madhkur), not on what is not mentioned (ghayr madhkur).
Here, 'talaq' is not mentioned in either the wife's or husband's speech.
3
Conclude the ruling
Since the word 'talaq' is absent, the husband's intention cannot effect divorce. Therefore, no talaq occurs.
This applies even if the husband had a strong intention to divorce.
4
Distinguish the explicit case
If the husband said 'I wish your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), it would be a valid divorce because 'talaq' is explicitly mentioned.
That would be an initiation (ibtida') of divorce, not a response.

اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت طالق ان شئت عورت نے کہا شنت ان شئت ۔ اور مرد طلاق کی نیت بھی کرتا ہے تو اس صورت میں امر بالید باطل ہو گیا۔ اور عورت کیلئے اختیار باقی نہیں رہا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کی طلاق کو مشیت غیر معلقہ پر معلق کیا ہے۔ اور عورت لائی مشیمت معلقہ یعنی اپنے چاہنے کو شوہر کے چاہنے پر معلق کیا ہے۔ پس شرط کے نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی اور چونکہ عورت لایعنی کام میں مشغول ہوگئی جو دلیل اعراض ہے۔ اس وجہ سے اس کے ہاتھ سے امر بھی نکل گیا۔ اور اختیار باقی نہیں رہا ( لا یعنی کام یہ ہے کہ عورت نے اپنی مشئیت کو شوہر کی مشیت پر معلق کیا ہے )۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر عورت کے قول شئتُ ان شئت کے جواب میں مرد نے شئت کہہ دیا تو اس لفظ شئت سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ اگر چہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہو۔ دلیل یہ ہے عورت کے کلام شئت ان شئت میں طلاق کا ذکر نہیں ہے تاکہ شوہر اسکی طلاق کو چاہنے والا ہوتا۔ اور اگر آپ کہیں کہ شوہر نے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو ہم جواب دیں گے کہ نیت کا عمل مذکور میں ہوتا ہے نہ کہ غیر مذکور میں ۔ اور یہاں نہ عورت کے کلام میں طلاق مذکور ہے اور نہ مرد کے کلام میں۔ اسلئے نیت کے باوجود طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ چنانچہ اگر شوہر نے شدت طلاقک کہا اور طلاق کی نیت بھی کی ہے تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ لیکن یہ طلاق کا واقع ہونا اسلئے نہیں کہ جواب ہے بلکہ اسلئے ہے کہ ابتداء طلاق واقع کی گئی۔
If the husband said to his wife: 'You are divorced if you wish,' and the woman said: 'I wish if you wish,' and the man also intends divorce, then in this case the command (amr bil-yad) becomes void, and no option remains for the woman. The proof is that the husband suspended his wife's divorce on an unconditional will (mashiyyah ghayr mu'allaqah), and the woman brought a conditional will (mashiyyah mu'allaqah), meaning she made her own will conditional on the husband's will. Thus, due to the condition not being fulfilled, divorce does not occur, and because the woman engaged in a futile act (laghw) which is evidence of aversion (i'rad), the command also leaves her hand, and no option remains. The author of Hidayah states: If in response to the woman's statement 'I wish if you wish,' the man said 'I wish,' then divorce does not occur from this word 'I wish,' even if the husband intended divorce. The proof is that in the woman's statement 'I wish if you wish,' there is no mention of divorce such that the husband would be intending her divorce. And if you say that the husband intended divorce, we will respond that intention operates on what is mentioned (madhkur), not on what is not mentioned. Here, divorce is neither mentioned in the woman's speech nor in the man's. Therefore, despite intention, divorce does not occur. However, if the husband said 'I wish your divorce' and also intended divorce, then divorce does occur. But this occurrence of divorce is not because it is a response, but because it is an initiation of divorce.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on conditional divorce when the wife makes her wish conditional on the husband's wish. The teacher explains: The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' (ant taliq in shi'ta). This is a suspended divorce (talaq mu'allaq) where the condition is the wife's will, and her will is unconditioned (mashiyyah mursalah). If the wife responds, 'I wish if you wish' (shi'tu in shi'ta), she introduces an additional condition (her wish is conditional on the husband's wish). Since the original condition (wife's wish) is not realized as an independent event, the divorce does not occur. Moreover, because the wife engaged in a futile act (laghw) by not complying properly, the option (amr bil-yad) that the husband had given her is voided. The text also addresses a scenario where the husband says 'I wish' in response. Even if he intended divorce, that word alone does not effect divorce because 'I wish' is not an expression of talaq. Talaq only occurs if the husband explicitly says 'I wish your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), which is a direct and immediate pronouncement. This distinction highlights the Hanafi principle that the word used must be explicit for divorce to take effect in such contexts.

Key Terms
مشیت معلقہ (mashiyyah mu'allaqah): A conditional will; one's wish made dependent on another condition.
امر بالید (amr bil-yad): The matter placed in the woman's hand; the option or authority given to her to pronounce divorce.
مذکور (madhkur): What is mentioned; the legal principle that intention operates only on explicitly stated words.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Hidayah, if the husband responds to the wife's 'I wish if you wish' by saying 'I wish' with the intention of divorce, what is the ruling?
A) Talaq occurs because of the intention.
B) Talaq occurs because 'I wish' is a response.
C) Talaq does not occur because 'I wish' is not a divorce expression.
D) Talaq occurs only if the wife also intends divorce.

Key Takeaway
A husband's statement of 'I wish' even with intent does not constitute divorce unless divorce is explicitly mentioned.

Difference Between 'Shi'tu Talaqaki' and 'Aradtu Talaqaki'
Explaining why 'I wished your divorce' effects talaq but 'I intended' does not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi linguistic principle to distinguish words that effect talaq (creation) from words that do not (mere intention).

Core Idea
‘I wished your divorce’ creates it; ‘I intended it’ only seeks it.

In Hanafi law, the effect of divorce depends on the precise wording used. A husband saying ‘shi’tu talaqaki’ terminates the marriage, but ‘aradtu talaqaki’ does not. This lesson explains the linguistic reasoning behind this critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases and their rulings
Note that the author explicitly states: 'shi'tu talaqaki' effects talaq, while 'aradtu talaqaki' does not. This is the starting point.
2
Trace the linguistic derivation of 'shi'tu'
Recognize that 'shi'tu' is derived from 'shay'' (thing), which means an existing entity. Thus 'shi'tu' means 'I brought into existence' (awjadtu). Bringing divorce into existence is equivalent to executing it (iqa').
3
Contrast with 'iradah' (intention)
'Iradah' means 'seeking' (talab), as in the hadith about seeking death. Seeking does not bring about the act. Therefore 'aradtu talaqaki' means 'I sought your divorce,' not 'I executed it.'
Seeking divorce does not constitute divorce.
4
Apply the principle
Only words that imply creation/execution of talaq effect it. Words indicating mere desire or intention are ineffective. This is a key Hanafi principle in divorce law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ شنت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی ۔ لیکن اردت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی ۔ وجہ فرق یہ ہے کہ شدت ماخوذ ہے شی ء سے اور شیء کے معنی ہیں موجود کے پس شمت ، اوجدت کے معنی میں ہوگا۔ اور ایجاد طلاق بغیر ایقاع کے نہیں ہوتا۔ اسلئے اس لفظ شئت طلاقک سے طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ اور ارادہ کے معنی ہیں طلب کے۔ جیسا کہ حدیث میں فرمایا گیا اٹھی زائد الموت ۔ پس اب اردت طلاقک کے معنی ہوں گے طلبت طلاقک اور یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ طلب طلاق سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوتی ۔ اسوجہ سے اس لفظ سے طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔
The author says: 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki) will effect divorce, but 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqaki) will not effect divorce. The reason for the difference is that 'shi'tu' is derived from shay' (thing), and shay' means an existing thing. Thus, 'shi'tu' means 'I brought into existence' (awjadtu). And bringing divorce into existence (ijad al-talaq) cannot happen without executing it (iqa'). Therefore, the word 'shi'tu talaqaki' effects divorce. As for 'iradah' (intention), its meaning is 'seeking' (talab), as mentioned in the hadith: 'Remove it, for seeking death (talab al-mawt).' So 'aradtu talaqaki' means 'I sought your divorce,' and it is established that seeking divorce does not effect divorce. Therefore, this word does not effect divorce.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a linguistic proof (talil) distinguishing between two similar phrases. The author of Hidayah explains that 'shi'tu talaqaki' (I wished your divorce) effects talaq because 'shi'tu' is derived from shay' (thing), meaning existence. Thus, it implies 'I created/brought about' the divorce, which is equivalent to actually pronouncing divorce. In contrast, 'aradtu talaqaki' (I intended your divorce) is derived from iradah (intention/will), which means 'seeking' or 'desiring' (talab), as seen in the hadith about seeking death. Seeking something does not bring it into existence. Therefore, expressing intention does not constitute divorce. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: words that imply creation/execution of talaq effect it, while words that only indicate desire or intention do not.

Key Terms
شِئْتُ (shi'tu): I wished/willed, but in this context interpreted as 'I brought into existence' (awjadtu) from shay'.
إرادہ (iradah): Intention or will, meaning 'seeking' (talab) in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the wife's permission is lacking.
B) Because 'aradtu' means 'I sought' (talab) which does not create divorce.
C) Because the husband must say it three times.
D) Because it is not said in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only words that imply creation/execution of talaq (like 'shi'tu') effect divorce; words of mere intention (like 'aradtu') are ineffective.

Ruling on 'Idha Shi'ta' and 'Mata Shi'ta' in Delegated Divorce
Hanafi ruling on tafwid with temporal phrases and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delegated divorce using temporal phrases and explain the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on 'idha shi'ta'.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, 'when you wish' grants ongoing authority; refusal is irrelevant and only one talaq is allowed.

This passage addresses a key question in Hanafi family law: when a husband delegates divorce to his wife using phrases like 'when you wish' or 'whenever you wish', does her authority expire immediately or continue? The ruling involves an internal Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Understanding this dispute clarifies the practical scope of delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize that the husband is delegating authority to the wife to divorce herself. The ruling is that the delegation is valid and effective immediately, giving the wife ongoing authority to pronounce one talaq at any future time.
The act is tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce).
2
Understand the treatment of 'mata shi'ta'
The word 'mata' (whenever) is purely temporal and general for all times. There is consensus that it grants ongoing authority, not limited to the sitting. If the wife refuses, her refusal is ineffective because the authority has already been placed in her hand.
Consensus (ijma) on 'mata' is mentioned in the text.
3
Analyze the ikhtilaf on 'idha shi'ta'
The word 'idha' (when) can be used for time or condition. The Sahibayn treat it like 'mata', granting ongoing authority. Abu Hanifa acknowledges its conditional use, but because the command has already entered her hand, doubt (shakk) does not remove the authority.
The key phrase: 'al-amr sar bi yadiha fala yakhruju bil shakk'.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, if the wife refuses immediately, the refusal might be considered valid because the condition might not be fulfilled. Under the Sahibayn, refusal has no effect.
The preferred view is that refusal is irrelevant, as in the Sahibayn's position.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The text favors the Sahibayn's view, treating 'idha' like 'mata'. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The passage mentions 'by consensus' for 'mata' and then treats 'idha' similarly for the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولوقال لها انت طالق اذا شئت او اذا ما شئت او متى شئت او متى ما شئت فردت الامر لم يكن رد اولا يقتصر على المجلس اما كلمة متى ومتى ما فلانها للوقت وهى عامة في الاوقات كلها كانه قال في ای وقت شئت فلايقتصر على المجلس بالاجماع ولوردت الامر لم يكن ردا لانه ملكها الطلاق في الوقت الذى شاءت فلم يكن تمليكا قبل المشية حتى يرتد بالرد ولا تطلق نفسها الا واحدة لانها تعم الازمان دون الا فعال فتملك التطليق في كل زمان ولا تملك تطليقا بعد تطليق واماكلمة اذاو اذاما فهى ومتى سواء عندهما وعند ابي حنيفة ان كان يستعمل للشرط كما يستعمل للوقت لكن الامر صار بيدها فلا يخرج بالشك و قدمر من قبل
If he said to her: 'You are divorced when you wish (idha shi'ta) or whenever you wish (idha ma shi'ta) or whenever you wish (mata shi'ta) or whenever you wish (mata ma shi'ta)', then she refused the command, it is not a refusal, and it is not restricted to the sitting. As for the word mata and mata ma, because they are for time, and they are general for all times, as if he said 'at any time you wish'. So by consensus it is not restricted to the sitting. And if she refused, it is not a refusal because he made her owner of divorce at the time she wishes; so it was not a delegation before the wish such that it would be rejected by refusal. And she cannot divorce herself except one, because the word is general for times, not for acts; so she owns the right to divorce in every time, but does not own a divorce after a divorce. As for the word idha and idha ma, according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) they are the same as mata; according to Abu Hanifa, although it is used for condition as well as time, but the command has entered her hand, so it does not leave due to doubt, and it has passed before.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) regarding delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq) using phrases that include 'idha shi'ta' (when you wish) and 'mata shi'ta' (whenever you wish). The ruling: If the husband uses any of these phrases, the wife is granted the authority to divorce herself at any time, and this authority is not limited to the immediate sitting (majlis). If she refuses or rejects this delegation, it is not valid because the husband has already made her the owner of divorce at the time she chooses; rejection before she wishes is meaningless. She can only give herself one talaq, because the words are general for time, not for number of acts. The ikhtilaf is about the word 'idha' and 'idha ma': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that they are the same as 'mata', giving ongoing authority. Abu Hanifa initially holds that 'idha' can be used for condition as well as time, but because the matter has already been placed in her hand, it does not get removed by doubt (shakk). The preferred view (mufta bihi) is likely the Sahibayn's opinion, as the text states 'by consensus' for mata and then treats idha similarly for the Sahibayn. The commentary notes that this discussion has been mentioned before.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation of authority to the wife to pronounce divorce herself.
متى (mata): Whenever; a time word indicating generality of time.
إذا (idha): When; can be temporal or conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must divorce herself immediately in the sitting.
B) She has the authority only for that one sitting.
C) She may divorce herself at any time in the future.
D) She may divorce herself multiple times.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, words of time like 'when you wish' grant the wife ongoing authority to give herself one talaq, and refusal is ineffective.

Effect of Words متى and إذا in Delegation of Talaq
Explaining the ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation of talaq using متى and إذا.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on delegation using 'mata' vs 'idha' and explain the practical difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
The word 'if' (إذا) in divorce delegation creates doubt, so authority stays limited to the sitting.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah examines the scope of authority given to a wife when a husband uses the words 'mata' (whenever) and 'idha' (if) in delegating divorce. While 'mata' grants unlimited time, 'idha' is interpreted differently by Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the rules of tafwid al-talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two word pairs
The passage contrasts 'متى/متى ما' and 'إذا/إذا ما'. Note that both pairs are used for delegation of talaq.
2
Determine the default ruling for 'متى'
When husband says 'انت طالق متى شئت', the wife can divorce at any time, not limited to the sitting. Because 'متى' indicates generality of time.
This ruling is agreed upon by all three imams.
3
State the Sahibayn's position on 'إذا'
They equate 'إذا' with 'متى', so the same unlimited authority applies.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold this view.
4
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position on 'إذا'
He argues that 'إذا' can also denote condition, causing doubt. Since authority is certain in the wife's hand, it will not be removed by doubt. Hence, authority is limited to the sitting.
The principle: 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk'.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, if wife leaves the sitting, she loses authority. Under Sahibayn, she can divorce at any future time.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد چہارم کتاب الطلاق اور وہ تمام اوقات میں عام ہے۔ گویا مرد نے انست طالق فى اى وقت شئت کہا۔ پس عموم وقت کی وجہ سے مجلس پر منحصر نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر عورت نے اپنا یہ اختیار رد کر دیا تو رو نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ شوہر نے عورت کو اسی وقت میں طلاق کا مالک بنایا ہے جس وقت میں وہ چاہے۔ پس چاہنے سے پہلے طلاق کا املک بنانا متفق ہی نہیں ہو گا۔ یہاں تک کہ عورت کے رد کرنے سے رد ہو جائے ۔ اور اس صورت میں عورت اپنے آپ کو ایک طلاق دے سکتی ہے۔ دلیل یہ کہ کلمہ متی اور منیٰ ما عموم زمانہ کیلئے آتا ہے نہ کہ عموم افعال کیلئے ۔ پس اس عورت کو عموم زمانہ کی وجہ سے ایک کے بعد دوسری طلاق دینے کا اختیار نہیں ہے۔ رہا کلمہ اذا اور اذا ما تو بہ اور متی صاحبین کے نزدیک دونوں برابر ہیں، لہذا جو حکم متی کا ہے۔ وہی اذا اور اذا ما کا ہوگا۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اذا اور اذا ما جس طرح وقت کیلئے استعمال ہوتے ہیں۔ اسی طرح شرط کیلئے بھی استعمال ہوتے ہیں ۔ پس شرط کیلئے استعمال ہونے کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اس صورت میں اختیار مجلس کے ساتھ خاص رہے اور وقت کیلئے استعمال ہونے کا مقتضی یہ ہے کہ مجلس برخواست ہوتے ہی اختیار عورت کے ہاتھ سے نکل جائے اور چونکہ امرعورت کے ہاتھ میں بالیقین ہے۔ لہذا شک کی وجہ سے نہیں نکلے گا۔
Ashraf ul Hidayah Urdu commentary of Hidayah volume four, book of talaq. And he is general in all times. It is as if the man said 'You are divorced whenever you wish'. So due to generality of time, it will not be restricted to the sitting. And if the woman rejected this authority, then it will not be effective. Because the husband made the woman the owner of divorce in whatever time she wishes. So before she wishes, making her owner of divorce is not established, until she rejects it. And in this case, the woman can give herself one divorce. The proof is that the word 'متى' and 'متى ما' come for generality of time, not for generality of actions. So she does not have authority to give divorce after another due to generality of time. As for the word 'إذا' and 'إذا ما', according to the Sahibayn, both are equal to 'متى', so the same ruling applies. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'إذا' and 'إذا ما' are used for time as well as for condition. So the requirement of being used for condition is that in this case the authority remains specific to the sitting, and the requirement of being used for time is that once she leaves the sitting, the authority leaves her hand. And since the authority is definitely in the woman's hand, it will not leave due to doubt.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage discusses the delegation of divorce using words like 'متى شئت' (whenever you wish) and 'إذا شئت' (if you wish). The author explains that when the husband says 'انت طالق متى شئت', the wife has the authority to divorce herself at any time, not limited to the sitting of utterance. This is because 'متى' indicates generality of time. If she rejects the authority before using it, the delegation is void. However, she can only give one divorce, not multiple, because 'متى' gives generality over time, not over actions. Then the author presents a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the words 'إذا' and 'إذا ما'. According to the Sahibayn, these words are equivalent to 'متى', so the same ruling applies: the authority is not limited to the sitting. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'إذا' and 'إذا ما' are used for both time and condition. Since they can denote condition, the authority is limited to the sitting (as in conditional divorce). But since they also denote time, there is doubt. As the authority is definitely in the woman's hand, it will not be taken away by doubt. Thus, the wife retains the authority only during the sitting, and if she leaves, it is lost.

Key Terms
متى (mata): A word meaning 'whenever', used for generality of time, not actions.
اذا (idha): A word used both for time and condition; its meaning is disputed among Hanafi scholars.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting where the statement is made; authority may be limited to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the ruling when a husband says 'انت طالق إذا شئت'?
A) The wife can divorce at any time.
B) The wife can only divorce in the sitting.
C) The wife cannot divorce.
D) The wife can divorce only once.

Key Takeaway
The word 'idha' in talaq delegation creates doubt, so under Abu Hanifa's view authority is limited to the sitting; the Sahibayn disagree, but the mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa.

Talaq — Divorce

Successive Divorces with the Word 'كلما'
Ruling on delegated divorce using 'كلما'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a wife's delegated authority to divorce herself when the husband says 'أنت طالق كلما شئت', and explain the scope, limits, and linguistic proof.

Core Idea
'كلما' allows successive divorces but only within the current marriage and one at a time.

When a husband says to his wife 'أنت طالق كلما شئت' (you are divorced whenever you wish), he delegates to her the power to divorce herself. This delegation hinges on the word 'كلما', which in Arabic implies repetition of an action each time its condition occurs. Hanafi jurists derive that she may divorce herself one after another up to three times, but cannot execute all at once, and the authority is tied to the existing marriage bond. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and its practical limits.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the delegated authority
When a husband says 'أنت طالق كلما شئت', he delegates to the wife the power to divorce herself. The key is the word 'كلما'. Understand that the authority is conditional on her wish and tied to the current marriage.
2
Analyze the linguistic effect of 'كلما'
'كُلَّما' implies repetition of an action each time its condition occurs. Here, each time she wishes, a divorce occurs. This is supported by the Quranic verse 'كلما نضجت جلودهم' (Quran 4:56), where 'كلما' indicates repeated roasting.
Hanafis treat 'كلما' as requiring separate occurrences.
3
Determine the maximum and sequence
Because 'كلما' necessitates individual occurrences, the wife can divorce herself one after another, up to three divorces (the maximum count). She cannot give all three in one utterance, as that would violate the requirement of repetition.
The text explicitly says 'ليس لها ان تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً في كلمة واحدة'.
4
Apply the restriction to current marriage
The delegation is tied to the current marriage (الملك القائم). If she remarries the same husband after a second marriage and tries to use the same delegation, it is ineffective because a new marriage creates a new ownership.
This resolves a potential question: why can't she use it again?

But the suspension refers to the existing ownership, so if the woman returns to the first husband after a second husband and then divorces herself, no divorce occurs because it is a new marriage. And it is not permissible for the woman to give herself three divorces in one utterance, because the word 'كلما' necessitates generality of individuals, not generality of collective, so she is not the owner of executing all at once and collectively. The answer is that the suspension refers to the existing ownership, meaning the husband entrusted to the woman only those divorces that are present in his ownership at that time. After a second husband, when she returns to the first husband and then divorces herself, no divorce will occur because this ownership arises from a new marriage. And the woman does not have the authority to effect three divorces upon herself with one utterance, because the word 'كلما' necessitates generality of individuals, not generality of collective. Therefore, the woman does not have the authority to effect at once and collectively. Some scholars are of the opinion that 'جملة' and 'جمعاً' have the same meaning. And some are of the opinion that 'جملة' is that the woman says 'I divorce myself three times' and 'جمع' is that the woman says 'I divorce myself one, one, one' – this opinion is apparent. Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a husband says 'انت طالق كلما شئت' (you are divorced whenever you wish). The word 'كلما' implies repetition of actions, so the wife can give herself divorces one after another, up to three. This is supported by the Qur'anic verse 'كلما نضجت جلودهم' (every time their skins are roasted). However, this delegation is tied to the current marriage (ملك قائم). If she remarries the same husband after a second husband and then tries to use the delegation, it is invalid because that is a new marriage. Also, she cannot give all three divorces in one utterance because 'كلما' requires individual occurrences, not collective action. The passage resolves a potential question: why can't she use the delegation again after remarrying the same husband? Because the delegation was for the existing ownership, not for a future marriage. Finally, there is a brief discussion on the meaning of 'جملة' and 'جمعاً' in the context of executing divorces simultaneously versus sequentially.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A word implying repetition of an action each time the condition occurs; used for successive acts.
ملك قائم (mulk qa'im): The existing ownership/marriage at the time of delegation; new marriage creates new ownership.

Place Words in Talaq Delegation
Explaining that saying 'حيث شئت' is treated as 'إن شئت' because place has no connection to talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why place words in talaq delegation are considered futile and explain the ruling when the husband says 'انت طالق حيث شئت'.

Core Idea
Place has no relation to divorce; 'where you wish' becomes 'if you wish', limiting the wife's option to the sitting.

When a husband delegates talaq using place words like 'حيث شئت' (wherever you wish), does the wife gain the right to choose any location? The Hanafi school rules that place words have no legal effect in divorce, so the delegation is treated as a conditional 'إن شئت' (if you wish). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The husband says 'انت طالق حيث شئت' or 'أين شئت'. Determine whether the mention of place affects the delegation.
Place words are nouns of place (haythu, ayna).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The wife is not divorced until she wishes, but her option is limited to the sitting. If she leaves the sitting, she loses the right.
This is the same as 'انت طالق إن شئت'.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is that divorce has no relation to place; a specific place cannot restrict divorce because if a divorce were tied to a place, it would occur in all places. Thus, the mention of place is futile.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) with conditional particles.
4
Understand the reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
Place words imply delay (like conditional particles), so they are metaphorically construed as 'if you wish'. This preserves the delayed nature of the delegation.
Unlike time words which are effective because divorce occurs at specific times.
5
Contrast with time words
Time words (e.g., 'متى') have a direct relation to divorce, so they are not futile. Place words are ignored.
This highlights the difference in treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

خیار باقی نہیں رہے گا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ کلمہ حیث اور این اسم مکان ہے۔ اور طلاق کسی مکان کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہوتی۔ چنانچہ اگر کسی مکان کے ساتھ مقید کر کے طلاق دی گئی تو وہ تمام مکانوں میں واقع ہوگی ۔ نہ کہ اس مخصوص مکان میں پس مکان کا ذکر لغو ہو گیا۔ اور مطلق مشعیت باقی رہی۔ گو یا شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے انت طالق ان شئت کہا۔ اور قیام عن انجلس کے بعد اس عورت کا خیار باقی نہیں رہے گا۔ جیسا کہ انت طالق ان شئت And if he said to her 'You are divorced where you wish' or 'wherever you wish', she is not divorced until she wishes, and if she left her sitting, she has no wish. Because the words 'حيث' and 'أين' are nouns of place, and divorce has no relation to place, so it becomes futile, and the absolute wish remains, so it becomes restricted to the sitting, contrary to time because time has relation to divorce, as it occurs in one time and not another, so consideration of particularity and generality is necessary. Translation: And if he said to the woman 'You are divorced where you wish' or 'at whatever place you wish', divorce will not occur until she wishes, and if she stood up from that sitting, she has no wish. Because 'حيث' and 'أين' are among nouns of place, and divorce has no relation to place. Therefore, the mention of place becomes futile, and the absolute wish remains; this wish will be restricted to the sitting. Contrary to time, because divorce has relation to time, so it occurs in one time and not another, so consideration of particularity and generality is necessary. Explanation of the issue: If the husband said to his wife 'You are divorced where you wish' or 'You are divorced if you wish', divorce will not occur until she wishes. And if she gets up from the sitting, her option is not retained. The proof is that 'حيث' and 'أين' are nouns of place, and divorce does not relate to any place. Consequently, if divorce is given while being tied to a specific place, it occurs in all places, not only that specific place, so the mention of place is futile. And the absolute wish remains, as if the husband said 'You are divorced if you wish'. And after standing up from the sitting, the woman's option is not retained, just as in the case of 'You are divorced if you wish', after standing up, the option is not retained. There is a question here: When in the mentioned issue the mention of place became futile, then 'You are divorced (if) you wish' remains. Just as 'You are divorced if you enter the house' immediately causes divorce, similarly 'You are divorced you wish' should also cause immediate divorce, because both lack a conditional particle. The answer is that 'حيث' and 'أين' both imply delay, even if a slight delay. And the conditional particle also implies delay. So both share in establishing the meaning of delay. Due to this suitability, 'حيث' and 'أين' are metaphorically construed as conditional particles. The conclusion is that 'You are divorced where you wish' and 'أين شئت' will mean 'You are divorced if you wish', and in the case of 'you wish', divorce [occurs] immediately.

Commentary
This passage addresses delegation using place words. When a husband says 'انت طالق حيث شئت' or 'أين شئت', the mention of place is considered futile because divorce is not tied to a place. Thus, it is treated as if he said 'انت طالق إن شئت' (you are divorced if you wish). Consequently, the wife has authority only during the sitting; if she leaves, the authority is lost. This differs from time words like 'متى' which have a connection to divorce timing. The passage also answers a potential question: why doesn't the talaq occur immediately like in conditional divorce 'إن دخلت الدار'? Because 'حيث' and 'أين' imply delay (similar to a conditional particle), so they are interpreted as conditionals, resulting in a delayed effect. The final sentence (cut off) indicates that when she wishes within the sitting, talaq occurs immediately.

Key Terms
حيث (haythu): A noun of place meaning 'where'; in delegation, it is considered futile and replaced by absolute wish.
إن (in): A conditional particle meaning 'if'; in delegation, it limits authority to the sitting.

Divorce Conditional on Place: The Hanafi Treatment of حيث شئت and أين شئت
Ruling on divorce tied to wife's choice of place

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school treats the phrase 'you are divorced where you wish' as a conditional delegation limited to the gathering, and distinguish it from time-based divorce phrases.

Core Idea
Place has no legal impact on divorce; its mention is void, leaving a conditional will valid only in the same sitting.

When a husband uses words like 'where you wish' to tie divorce to a place, the Hanafi school holds that the place is irrelevant. Instead, the phrase is treated as a delegation of the divorce decision to the wife, but only if she decides within that same gathering. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and contrasts it with time-based conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Phrase and Its Literal Meaning
The husband says 'You are divorced where you wish' (حيث شئت) or 'wherever you wish' (أين شئت). Literally, these words refer to a place, but the ruling considers their legal effect.
The exact wording matters for interpretation.
2
Recognize the Irrelevance of Place to Divorce
Divorce is not connected to location; it is a temporal event. Therefore, mentioning a place is legally void (لغو). The word for place drops out, leaving only 'you wish' – a conditional delegation.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) argument: place has no causal link to divorce.
3
Apply the Metaphor: Treating 'Where' as 'If'
Because 'where' implies a delay (just like a condition), it is treated metaphorically as the conditional particle 'if' (إن). The phrase thus means 'You are divorced if you wish.'
This is based on the suitability (munasaba) between place words and conditional delay.
4
Define the Scope: Option Limited to the Gathering (Majlis)
In the conditional 'if you wish' (إن شئت), the wife's option expires when she leaves the sitting. The same applies here: if she leaves, her option is lost.
The majlis is the critical time window; after it, the divorce does not occur.
5
Contrast with Time-Based Phrases
Time has a genuine connection to divorce (e.g., 'tomorrow' or 'at any time'). Therefore, time specifications are given effect, unlike place which is void.
Example: 'You are divorced tomorrow' is valid and immediate, while 'you are divorced where you wish' is conditional.
